COURT-MARTIAL FOR STOESSEL.
Announcement Arouses Indignation of
Russian Populace.
ST. PETERSBURG, Jan. s.âFew
incidents of the whole war have
aroused more bitter criticism than
the blunt announcement, officially is
sued by the General Staff, that Gen
eral Stoessel will have to come home
and stand court-martial for surrend
ering the fortress of Port Arthur.
While this is an ancient regulation
and quite according to law, it is bit
terly resented on all sides that such
an announcement should have been
gratuitously made in the same bul
letin containing General Stoessel's ap
peal to the Emperor for "lenient
judgment on a garrison reduced to
shadows, who have done all that was
possible for human beings to uphold
the honor of Russia in the face of her
enemies." The Novoe Vremya. despite
the example made by the suspension
of the Rush yesterday, says:
By all means let us have a court-martial
â¢s>d make it. if possible, severe. The cruel
.Judge will, perhaps, deal leniently with those
who have *iven their blood and lives for their
country. Perhaps, also, the .court will deter
mine why a fortress known to be threatened
with blockade is not supplied with necessary
food and munitiona to enable It to hold out.
Perhaps such a court will bring- to light many
dark, hidden things and expose the creeping,
underground enemies of Russia who are In
finitely more dangerous to the nation than
those v.-ho fight in the open.
There is no definite information
here regarding General Stoessel's con
dition, but it has been public gossip
for some time that he is suffering
from cancer.

RELIEF SHIP STOPPED.
I Japanese Turn Back British Cruiser
Carrying Supplies.
WEIHAIWEI, Jan. 5.-The British
| cruiser Andromeda, which sailed from
> here yesterday for Port Arthur with
| hospital stores and surgeons to assist
; the sick and wounded, returned here
I to-day, not having been allowed to
j make a landing at Port Arthur. The
cruiser proceeded to a small bay ten
miles north of Port Arthur, where she
was met by some Japanese. The latter
refused all assistance or stores of any
kind and declined to allow the- ship*s
officers 10 approach closer, the reason
given being the presence of unlocated
mines.
i'HEFU, Jan. s.âJapanese Consul
Mizouno to-night notified Russian Con
sul Tiedemann that the first of the 1000
I Russian non-combatants at Port Ar
| thur who are to come to Chefu will ar
rive on Friday. It will be some time
before the entire 1000 reach here, owing
to a shortage of transportation facili
ties.
The authorities at Tokio having re
fused a proposition made by the Rus
sian Minister in Peking, Paul Lessar,
to send a ship laden with medical sup
plies and food for the invalids, it has
been suggested that this ship be divert -
jpd to ('hefu for the benefit of the non-
I combatants.
Consul Mizouno says that the refusal
of the Japanese to allow either the An
dromeda or the ship'proposed by Min
ister Lessar to visit Port Arthur is in
pursuance of a strict policy not to al
low any foreign vessel to approach the
harbor. It is understood, however, that
the refusal of the Japanese to accept
the aid tendered by the Andromeda
was an error born of their desire to
show to the world that Japanese hu
manitarianism was fully equal to the
occasion.
Sympathy for the sick and wounded
Russians at Port Arthur is widespread,
but the suggestion that they be cared
for at Chefu, Tientsin, Shanghai, Wei
haiwei and Tsingtau has led to an in
quiry which developed the fact that the
combined facilities of the places named
are equal to harboring only a very
small percentage of them. The general
opinion here Is that the great majority
of the sick and wounded will be com
pelled to remain at Port Arthur until
they are sufficiently convalescent to
return to Russia.

Bard's, Managers Expect to Bring
About a Conference When He Ar
rives From Nation's Capital.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. s.âThe dullest
Senatorial campaign ever conducted in
Sacramento is just beginning to give
some signs of animation. At last the
members of the Legislature from the
south comprehend that if they do not
speedily adjust their differences of
opinion and consolidate their entire
forces in the support of one candidate
the legislators from Central and North
ern California will hand the toga to
George A. Knight or Arthur G. Fisk.
There are inaications to-night that
measures wiil be taken soon to bring
together all the conflicting forces in
the ranks of southern Republicans. Ac
cessions to the Knight column have a
tendency to accelerate the negotiations.
There is elation at Knight s iuad
quarters and depression in another
camp over a report, which is generally
accepted as authentic, that James Gil
lis, State Librarian, has captured and
transferred to Knight the Sacramento
delegation of three membersâSenator
James >A. McKee and Assemblymen
Charles O. Busick and Frank J.
O'Brien. Oldtiir.ers assert that a Sac
ramento delegation can see a railroad
programme earlier, catch it quicker
and hold it longer than can any other
aggregation of statesmen in the land.
Hence some special significance is at
tached to the attitude t>f the Sacra
mento representatives. All this leadd
to a surmise that the gilded brick which
Herrin has molded will bear the initials
"F. P. F.\" instead of "G. A. K."
The Flint boomers do not conteniDlate
the candidacy of Knight with serene
complaisance. They complain with
some degree of justice that their candi
date has been hammered because Jere
Burke, John Mackenzie and Walter
Parker of the Southern Pacific political
staff are espousing Flint's candidacy,
and after the hammering another aspi
rant for the Senatorial toga succeeds to
the favor of the corporation. Judge
Dibble, who is here as George Knight's
chief of staff, insists that Herrin is
keeping "hands off" as between Flint
and Knight, but the railroad blanket of
neutrality does not cover Senator Bard,
who is exposed to the merciless ele
ments of corporate disfavor. Bard has
done something or there is apprehen
sion that he will do something to incur
the displeasure of Herrin. Lynch.
Burke,' Parker and Mackenzie are ex
pected to hammer Bard until he is
driven from the field and then Herrin
will step in and say to the Republicans
of the Legislature, "My hands are off.
You can give the Senatorshio to mv
candidate of the north or my candi
date of the south."
When Senator Bard arrives in Sacra
mento steps will be taken to bring
about a conference of the leading Re
publicans of Southern California.
Frank P. Flint openly asserts that he
would sacrifice his own personal ambi
tions rather than that the south should
lose the Senator. Henry T. Oxnard
stands ready to cast the weight of his
influence for harmony on the Senator
ship in the south. It is said that Sen
ator Bard, whose ambitions are not in
tense, will be as magnanimous as his
competitors are. The importance of
harmony and a consolidation of all the
Republican forces in the south were
topics dwelt upon at a conference be
tween Dr. Rowell and Oxnard this aft
ernoon.

CONGRESS
BALKS THE
PRESIDENT
No Railroad Rate
Legislation at
Present
Special Dispatch to Tbe Call.
CALL BUREAU. POST BUILDING,
WASHINGTON, Jan. 6.âRailroad rate
legislation is dead, so far as the pres
ent' session of Congress is concerned.
It was said by leaders in both branches
to-day that nothing could be done, no
matter how much President Roosevelt
may desire it or how much he may
urge It.
The collapse of the campaign for gov
ernment supervision of railroad rates
takes away from the present session
I the only real live question which prom-
I ised at one time to keep Congress agi
tated. When the President gave up all
j hope of having something done in the
â  line of tariff revision, he had reason to
believe something might be accom
plished in railroad legislation. But this
has now gone the way of tariff revi
{ sion.
! The present situation has been
! brought about by an utter failure to
I come to an agreement on the form of
legislation. The belief was expressed.
! both in administration circles and in
I Congress, that something should be
done at once to remedy the evil point
ed out in the President's message, but
only on this point was there any agree
ment. When it came to methods, not to
say the phraseology of the bill to be
presented, there were almost as many
different views as members of the
Cabinet and leaders in Congress.
Senator Elkins had another confer
ence with the President to-day, but on
coming from the White House he
would not say that there was any
chance for action on a railroad bill in
the near future.
ANUARY 6, 1905.
THE River Improvement and Drainage Association of California reached a unanimous agreement yes
terday regarding a plan for the improvement of the Sacramento and San Joaquin rivers and the
reclamation of i ,000,000 acres of land that annually are menaced by floods. It was estimated that
when reclamation is permanently affected the value of $ 100,000,000 will be added to the taxable property
ot the State. The report of a board of engineers was accepted as a working basis.
FINDS NEW
SATELLITE
OF JUPITER
Important Discov
â¢ cry at Lick Ob
servatory.
Special Dispatch to Tbe CalL
SAN JOSE, Jan, s.âLick Observatory
has discovered a sixth satellite of Jupi- ]
ter. The discovery was made by Pro
fessor Perrine by means of the Cross
ley reflector and is the result of a sys
tematic search begun early in De
cember.
On photographs taken at that time
of the region west of Jupiter the image
of an unknown body, which changed
its position nightly, was found by Per
rine. Since then investigations have
been carried on to decide whether it
was a satellite or one of the asteroids.
During the present week observations
have been made which leave no doubt
that the body is revolving around the
planet. Last night it was forty-five
minutes of an arc due west of the
planet and the distance is now increas
ing slowly.
The satellite is of the fourteenth
magnitude, and to detect it requires a
telescope as large as ten or twelve
inches in diameter.
The orbit of this moon cannot be de
termined until observations extending
over many weeks have been obtained.
However, Professor Perrine says it is
five or six times farther from the
planet than is the outermost of the five
satellites previously known, and it is
possible that the period of revolution
around the planet is from six to eight
months.
The observations that led to the dis
covery of the satellite were the first
made with the Crossley reflector since
it has been provided with a modern
mounting. This Is the second satellite
discovered at Lick Observatory.
Jupiter's four very bright satellites
were discovered by Galileo in 1610, but

GOES AFTER

Personal Inquiry to
Be Conducted in
Chicago.
Special Dispatch to Th* Call.
CHICAGO. Jan. s.âFollowing the
charges made by Attorney General
Moody that the packers were in a con
spiracy to control the market of the na
tion for fresh meat, it was learned to
day that Secretary Garfield of the De
partment of Commerce and Labor is
coming to Chicago to begin an investi
gation in person. The packers are much
agitated over the news.
It is said Secretary Garfield is* anx
ious to have the Federal courts nass
on the question of whether he can com
pel the packers to produce their books
and submit to* direct examination on
the witness stand as to the methods
they follow in business.
At the annual meeting of the stock
holders of Swift & Co. to-day Presi
dent Lewis F. Swift said:
"It is needless for me to say to those
present, who are more or less familiar
with the facts, that the reports In cir
culation about the 'beef trust' are
without foundation. There is absolute
ly no conspiracy or combination to con-
S trol either the purchase of livestock or
the sale of meats or packing-house
products. On the other hand, the Dack
lng industry is subject to the severest
kind of competition, both in buying
and selling, and the per cent of profit
on sales in the packing Industry is less
than in other lines of business lust
now."
It was not until nearly 300 years after
ward, on September 9. 1892, that Pro
fessor Â£. E. Barnard discovered the
fifth with the Lick telescope. The fifth
and sixth satellites are of approximate
ly the same brightness.

PRICE FIVE CENTS.
Steps Taken
to Interest
Legislature
Co-operation of the
Federal Power
Is Sought.
COST CONSIDERED
Important Meeting
Is Held at the
Palace.
HARMONY IN WORK
Unanimous Action
Is Taken After
Discussion.
The River Improvement and Drain
age Association of California, at a
meeting held yesterday at the Palace
Hotel, unanimously adopted a report of
its executive committee, in which a
plan was embodied for carrying out the
great work which is advocated by the
association. It was set forth in the re
port that 1,000,000 acres of land now
subject to ravages by floods in the val
leys' of California, by the rivers, can be
reclaimed, and that the increased value
of the land, for purposes of taxation,
will yield to the State an annual added
revenue of 1300,000. These statements
were based upon the report of a com
mission of engineers who visited the
great interior valleys and inspected the
rivers and their watersheds. The engi
neers are T. G. Dabney, Henry B. Rich
ardson, M. A. Nurse and H. M. Chit
tenden.
The report of the commission of engi
neers was before the River Improve
ment and Drainage Association in
pamphlets. One paragraph is as fol
lows:
"The direct benefits to the entire Sac
ramento Valley as the result of perfect
ing the plan of improvement in the
reclamation of about 1,000.000 acres of
extremely fertile land and placing it In
a position of assured safety from over
flow, together with direct and indirect
advantages to many associated inter
ests, must be expressed in a moneyed
valuation reaching at least $100,000,000,
which will assuredly justify the cost of
the work required to bring about these
results."
It was estimated that the works pro
posed by the board of engineers will
cost something like $24,000,000. The
works are, as planned, to consist of
channel rectification and pnlarsrement
by the point action of natural and me
chanical agencies and also the gradual
concentration and confinement of the
volume of floods of the rivers and the
lesser streams of the Sacramento Val
ley between lines of levees so located
"as to ultimately provide ample chan
nel area for transporting all the flood
volume."
In the meantime, until such channel
rectification shall have been secured,
the engineers suggest that "relief will
be afforded the levees by introduction
of escapement weirs to temporary by
pass channels for conducting the floods
through the basins."
Concerning the engineering plans
there was no discussion at yesterday's
meeting. Some talk took place regard
ing the means proposed by the execu
tive committee of the River Improve
ment and Drainage Association to se
cure funds to carry on the great works.
COMMITTEE SUGGESTIONS.
The executive committee suggested,
briefly expressed, that the lands within
the drainage district should pay one
third of the cost, the State to pay one
third and the Federal Government one
third, but also recommended that a
method of officially determining the
cost to be borne by the different inter
ests should be provided. Nothing can
be done, so it was set forth, to affect
the regimen of the rivers without the
consent of the Federal Government,
and Joint action of the State and Fed
eral Governments should be secured as
the first step. Therefore it was pro
posed by the executive committee that
the Legislature should be asked to rec
ommend to Congress the adoption of a
joint resolution authorizing the ap
pointment of a board of three engineers
of the United States army to examine
and report upon the report recently
submitted by the board of engineers
that viewed the Sacramento and San
Joaquin rivers.
A minority report was filed by Charles
Wesley Reed and E. A. Walcott. Reed
made the principal speech in support of
the contention in the minority report
that the way to proceed directly was
to have a constitutional amendment
submitted to the people in regard to the
action that will be necessary to raise
the State's share of the more than $20,
--000,000 to be expended. This caused
some rather warm discussion, but in
the end, after the minority report had
been rejected, Reed and Walcott voted
to make the action of the association

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL. FRIDAY. JANUARY (>, 11)05.
APPORTION THE BURDEN
PARTIES CONCERNED
ARE TO CARRY.
SHARE OF EACH INTEREST IS ONE THIRD
Commissions Are Provided For to Con
duct Operations on Streams in Detail.
The report of the executive commit
tee of the River Improvement and
Drainage Association of California is
given in full herewith. The principal
points contained in it refer to the cost
and apportionment of . the work to be
performed and contain a declaration
that there can be no question that the
burden must be divided between the
Federal and State governments and the
land owners; sots up that Fed
eral Government belongs the "preserva
tion of the navigability of the rivers
and the restoration of channels and to
prevent the escape of water from the
rivers; estimates the gain that will
accrue to the State under a great
reclamation plan: recommends that
one-third of the expense shall be car
ried by the Federal Government, one
third by the State and one-third by
the property owners; adopts the recom
mendation of the engineers as to the
basis of river improvement; provides
for a drainage district to include with
in its'boundaries the territory shown
in the map submitted by the engineers;
also provides for a board of river con
trol of two members to have charge
of the construction of levees and chan
nels In connection with the disposition
of flood waters and their care; also a
drainage commission of eleven mem
bers who will appoint assessors
to assess upon the land with
in the drainage district charges
proportioned to benefits; asserts
that it is of great importance that
the entire funds needed shall be guar
anteed from the start; recommends
that any appropriation of money by the
State shall be conditional upon appro
priation by the Federal Government;
urges immediate action upon the ex
ecutive committee.
The text in full of the executive com
mittee's report is as follows:
Your executive committee, appointed at the
oioee of the convention held In San fÂ«nclac?.
May 28 and 24 last, begs to submit the fol
lO furt'herance n ln of the resolutions passed at
the convention three eminent engineers resid
ing out ot the State of California were ap
pointed to submit n comprehensive plan of
river Improvement, the State engineer co-op
erating with theÂ»c three gentlemen.
The State Is to be congratulated on the
character of the cm mission of engineers thus
organized, and your commute* does not hesi
tate to express its fullest belief that the re
port presented by this commission cannot fail,
if carried out in its essential features, to solve
effectively ana permanently the problem or
river reform.
In anticipation of this report your commit
tee has spent much time and effort in the en
deavor to arrive at an equitable and Just ap
portionment of the estimated cost of the
work. '
That the burden roust be divided between
the Federal and State governments and the
landowners there can be no Question.
The navlgatton pf the Sacramento and San
Joaquin rivers is a matter purely within the
jurisdiction of the Federal Government, and its
I protection is a burden that the Federal Gov-
I rnment has in ail cases assumed. At an early
period the Sacramento River was susceptible
of ravtgatlon throughout it? entire course in
the Sacramento Valley and the Feather River
was navigated as far as Marysvllle. From
various or.uses the channels of these rivers
have been obstructed and filled with sediment.
Both the navigability and the flood carrying
capacity ot the rivers have been seriously im
paired This condition has arisen from the
failure of the State and Federal governments
ito protect and maintain the channels, and in
iustlee to the State of California the Federal
Government should contribute to the Joint
fund it is hoped to create, the object of which
will be not only to lestore the channels to the
\ condition they were In before impairment, but
I add to their capacity to such an extent as
1 shall justify reclamation of the lands through
which thoy flow.
To do this, and maintain them in future,
the report of the engineers shows plainly the
work necessary to be done. It will be neces
sary to prevent the escape of water from the
river and to concentrate the flow within the
channels in order to provide sufficient current
Ito remove the sediment which has already
: accumulated and to prevent deposit in the
| future.
| There, ia an additional burden that should
j be borne by the Federal Government, and that
jlÂ» the correction and improvement of the
I channels, so aa to make them more servlce-
J able for navigation.
Commerce i.Â« rapidly increasing and the
I valleys are becoming more productive, and
I this calls for some consideration on the part
lot the Federal Government to make provi
sions for moving the product. The burden of so
i doing is one that the Federal Government has
; assumed throughout the United States, and
we can see no reason why it should not be
adopted on the Pacific Coast.
STATE IS INTERESTED.
The State is interested In this work for
j many reasons. The lands already partially
I reclaimed within the valleys will "be saved
I from recurrence of the disastrous flood? that
i have heretofore periodically devastated them,
j A large area of land that is now useless and
! unoccupied will be placed In a condition to
j make its reclamation possible, will afford
homes for increasing population and add to
the products of the State to an enormous ex
: tent. It is estimated that 1.000.000 acres ot
the richest land in California can be safely
ana permanently reclaimed and an average of
; WW- an acre added to its value. The reeiama-
I tion of some of this land has already bÂ»en
' attempted, but part of its has been allowed
'to lapse entirely and more of it la subjected
|to occasional overflow and an insignificant
I proportion only can be looked upon aa being
I permanertly reclaimed. â¢
The increased value of the land for pur
i poses of taxation at the present rate of tax
, ation on the land alone would add to the
revenue of the State- at least $250,000 annually,
iln addition to this opening of the land to
settlement and cultivation will necessarily re
quire the erection of improvements upon the
land and the acquisition of personal property
for the purpose of cultivation and manage
ment. This will easily add another $50,000 to
1 the revenues of the State, making a total of
; $;)00,000 per annum that the State will de
j rive in benefits âand this benefit will be per
petual.
Indirectly the State will gain largely from
I the progress of so Important a work. The
1 entire coat of the work will be spent right
here in California; employment will be found
for ft large force of men. a large fleet of
dredgers will have to be built and operated.
Vast quantities of fuel will be required for
power; freighting supplies will of iteelf be ft
.large Item. Subsequently the magnificent ter- 1
ritory developed, all susceptible of intense and
varied.cultivation, will be sending through the
channels of commerce o splendid contribution
to the product of the State.
LAND OWNERS' SHARE.
A portion of the expense should undoubtedly
Ibe paid by assessment upon the lands to be
i benefited, for the reason that they will be
enhanced In value.
A somewhat exaggerated estimate of the
probable enhancement has become current. It
is sometimes suggested that a great part of
â  tha cost of the proposed work should be borne
by the lands because of the greatly Increased
value to be given to them. Values of $100 and
$160 an acre are sometimes talked of. and it
Is forgotten that the high value of some re
claimed tracts of thia State has been reached
by added improvements of houses, barns, good
roads and tbe growth of important settlements,
with their hotels, poatofflcea, etc. Moreover
the high estimation of the value of lands la
rather an anticipation of the value It may
have when we have ft large population and
assured markets for the product of intenae
cultivation. But wÂ« think the price that Is
realized In tbe aelling of large tracts of land
cornea approximately near establishing their
value and the following statement of facta
should be considered before accepting; tha more
picturesque estimate of value:
The upper division of Roberta Island, con
tatnlnrfl 12,000 acres, was cold In farms In
1888-1889 after reclamation, at an average
price of $30 per acre. â¢
The lower division of Roberta Island, con
taining 13.000 acre*, was sold in farms la
1892-1897 after reclamation, at an average
price of $80 per acre.
Some 4000 acres of land on Grand Island
were sold in farms between the years 1890
--| 1901. after reclamation, at prices averaging $00
, per acre.
The land is undoubtedly lntrinaically worth
more than the prices detailedâthat Is to say.
It will pay Interest on higher valuations, but
the element of risk recognized is a potent fac
tor in determining Its selling value.
VALUE TO BE ADDED.
A conservative estimate of tho additional
value to be added to the lands by complete
reclamation is $50 per acre. Thia at first
would seem to be such an advantage that the
lands could well afford to bear a large pro
portion of the burden, but we must bear in
mind that this Increased valuation will not be
produced solely by the expenditure of the
money contemplated by the report of the en
gineers. When ail the work has been done, as
contemplated by the report, the lands will still
he only partially reclaimed, or to be more ex
act, this work is to-Justify the further work
necessary to reclaim the lands. There will be
an additional expense pumping plants, etc..
the entire coat of which will fall upon the land
owners.
While It |g Just that the Federal Government
should restore the original natural condition
of the channels, and that the State. In pursu
ance of iv undertaking with the Federal Gov
ernment to reclaim the awamp lands, should
supplant this with such additional work as
sliuuhl give control of flood water, still It must
be constantly borne in mind that to Create con- i
dltlons which will render possible the recla
mation of private lands by their owners at
their own expense Is all that can be asked of
either, and everything for direct land reclama
tion must be eliminated from the estimates
before determining the share of the burden the
State and Federal governments shall be asked
to bear.
, PLAN OF ORGANIZATION.
For the execution of the work and the ad
ministration of the funds, your committee rec
ommends the tullowlng plan of organization:
That the recommendations of the engineers
appointed by the State bu adopted as the basis
of river Improvement.
That a drainage district be formed at the
prevent session nf the Legislature to include
within its boundaries the territory shown by
the map of the commission of engineers.
That a board oi rtVSf control ite created,
consisting of two mrniliers, one selected by the
State of California and one by the Drainage
Commission hereinafter mentioned. The board
of river control to have charge of the con
struction of such leveen and channels as are
intended to do duty in disposing of flood wat
ers, and thereafter control the said levees and
channels, such hoard of river control to act In
conjunction with such board or officer aa may
be hereafter designated by the Federal Gov
ernment.
The member of said board of river control
selected by the State to be a civil engineer.
A drainage commission for the district con
sisting of eleven members shall be elected by
the land owners of said district: three from
Sacramento COUBty, two from San Joaquin
County, one from Placer and Yuba Jointly, one
from Glenn and Butte jointly, one from Sut
ter, one from Colusa, one from Yolo, one fr*Â»m
Solano.
TCaeh bona fide owner of lands in. the district
shall be entitled to vote In iierson or by proxy
for the election of members of tbe drainage
commission at meetings to be held at desig
nated places every four years and shall have
the right to cast one vote for each $1 worth of
real estate Owned by him or her in the dis
trict, the value thereof to be determined from
the next prcedlng assssment roll of the county,
and a majority of the votes cast at such elec
tion shall elect.
I.and belonging to the estate of any Infant
owner may be represented b' - the guardian of
the estate or such Infant, and that belonging
to the estate of any deceased owner by his
executor or administrator.
The drainage commission shall apporftt three
assessors wljo shall not be owners of or Inter
ested in land embraced In the district.
THE LAND.
The Assessors shall assess upon the land
within the district a charge proportionate to
the estimated cost of the work according to
the benefits to be derived therefrom and shall
make a list of the charges so assessed against
the land in each county separately; the As
sessors shall return said lists to the drainage
commission, which shall appoint a time and
;>lace in each county and give notice thereof
In some newspaper published in said county
for a hearing. At such hearing any landhold
er dissatisfied with the assessment may ap
pear before the ucmmlssion and file objections
thereto. '?h a commission shall take evidence.
If any is offered, and shall decide upon the
merits of the objections so made and the de
cision of the commission shall be final.
The drainage commission shall have power
to form new reclamation districts within said
drainage district and change the boundaries
of any district when necessary or desirable for
the promotion of better reclamation.
They shall have charge and control of all
matters of reclamation In said district affect-
Ini; the public safety and welfare, outside of
those placed in charge of the board of river
control, and excepting those conferred upon
the trustees of reclamation districts.
All existing reclamation districts to be con
tinued as they are now formed, until changed
by the Drainage Commission. Those now ex
isting and those hereafter created to nave
local Jurisdiction aa now provided by law,
excepting so far as such Jurisdiction may be
affected by powers conferred upon the Board
of Rtver Control and upon the Drainage Com
mission.
The i ost of constructing the works that are
to be placed in charge of the Board of River
Control to be paid as follows: One-third by
assessment upon the lands within the drainage
district: one-third by the State, and one-third
by the Federal Government.
The assessment to be placed upon the lands
to v be apportioned thereon acordlng to the bene
fits received; that is to say."work exclusively
benefiting any particular locality must be as
sessed solely upon the land situated therein.
All the money collected from assessments
upon the lands shall be deposited in the State
treasury.
NEED ENTIRE FUND.
We strongly urge that the recommendation
of the engineers that '"the entire funds for
the work should be guaranteed from the
start" is of great Importance, to prevent pos
sibility of the work being commenced and
money expended that would produce no re
sults unless the work were completed.
Aay appropriation made by the State shall
be conditional upon appropriation by the Fed
eral Government and assessment on the lands
of an additional sum sufficient' to complete
the work, and all assessments levied upon land
of the district shall be made available and
only on the condition that the State and Fed
eral governmenta shall make appropriations
sufficient, to furnish tbe additional funds to
meet the entire coat. â
While we recommend the foregoing specific
i division of the cost, we recognize that there
are too many conflicting opinions to permit
a hasty conclusion and therefore suggest a
method of officially determining the propor
tion ot coat to be borne by tbe different in
terests. â¢ â¢ . .
As the State can do absolutely nothing af
fecting the regime of the rivers without the
consent of the Federal authorities and *s. in
any event. Joint , action by the State and Fed
eral governments is essential' to the solution
of th- problem, the first step must necessarily
be" to secure auch co-operative action.
To that end we recommend that the executive
> committee be- authorised to memorialize tbe
I Legislature In the name of the association,
,to recommend to Congress the passage of a
joint resolution appointing a board consisting
of three engineers of the Cnited States army
for the purpose of making an examination
and report on the report recently submitted
by a commiaÃion of engineers appointed by
the State of California, for the improvement
of the Sacramento 'and San Joaquin rivers and
eatimating the cost of restoring and improving
the -original navigable capacity of said rivers.
Such project shall Include all necessary rec
tification, straightening, deepening and widen
ing of said stream, as well as the construction
iof any levee system or systems that may be
I required to rectify the river and provide means
â of escape of the flood wave to tidewater, hay
, Ini in view not only the interests of naviga
tion, but also those of flood control and rec
lamation.
Said board, so far as practicable, shall work
In harmony with any engineers, commissioners
or officers representing the State of California.

JENNINGS' ADDRESS.
At the opening of the proceedings
yesterday President Rufus P. Jennings
made an address. In part he said:
"It is impracticable at this time to
go into all of the details of the work !
that has been done by the commitee. j
The necessity and importance of j
prompt action, however, is realized, '
and that the comprehensive report of '

danger and the sooner this danger is
averted the better. The points of con
troversy that have heretofore divided
forces and probably delayed conclusive
actions are all, I hope, finally disposed

engineers. Any one who studies care
fully the argument made by these
gentlemen which they arrive at their
final conclusions cannot fall to see that
most of the remedies previously sug
gested had their measure of jnerit. I
This shows what all of us have recog- '
nized that they were the conclusions j
of thinking men who were probably
not furnished with the elaborate de
tails from which the commission of, en
gineers worked.
seemsÂ» to me good reason has !
been shown why all controversial i
points should be abandoned, and aban- j
doned forever. Never again must our ;
efforts bÂ» weakened by discussing the
several issues that have been settled by I
the report of the commission of en- j
gineers.
"There is much yet to be done. The
work has only commenced. That which i

which i:i its benefits to the State are i

for one."

WASHINGTON OFFICIALS
WILL PASS ON THE BIDS
Proposals Are Received at Phoenix
for Supplying Reclamation Ser
vice With Fuel Oil.
PHOENIX, Ariz., Jan. s.âBids were
opened at the headquarters of the
reclamation service here to-day for
furnishing 50,000 barrels of fuel oil
for the Roosevelt dam and they will
be referred to Washington for the
awarding. There were various propo
sitions submitted, including oil at the
supply point, or delivered at Roose
velt, at Mesa City and Globe, the near
est railroad points, and also on the
wagon haul. The lowest quotation at
the supply point was by the Union
Oil Company at 19 9-10 cents a barrel
at Bakersfleld. The lowest bid of oil
delivered at the dam was by C. R.
Eager & Co.. Los Angeles, of $3 48.
The lowest for delivery at Mesa City

WOMAN MISSING
Friends of Mrs. Augusta Dispute Over Custody of
Bishop Fear She Was Vie- Mrs. Chadwick Is Settled
tim of Colorado Murderer I by Federal Authorities
FIND NO TRACE OF HER
Anxiety Not Relieved by
Seeming Identification by
Mrs. fouton's Relatives
Special Dispatch to The Call.
DENVER, Jan. 6.âMrs. Augusta I.
Bishop, who came to Denver early last
year from San Francisco, may have
been the woman murdered on Mount
Cutler, near Colorado Springs, whose
body was found on the mountain on
December 17. Her friends In this city
are certain that ahe answers the de
scription of the dead woman In every
way, and are not shaken by the seem
ingly positive identification of the body
as that of Mrs. Bessie Bouton of Syra
cuse, N. Y.
Mrs. Bishop has not been heard from
since last June. Shortly before that
time she went to Colorado Springs. She
was engaged In selling hair and face
preparations, and when she left Den
ver about $300 worth of various prepa
rations were left in this city in the care
of Mrs. L. U. Jones, with whom she had
stopped. Mrs. Jones has not heard from
her and still has the goods. Mrs. Bish
op promised to return not later than
October. Her general description close
ly answers that of the victim of the
mysterious tragedy. Her hair was the
same color and her weight and age the
same.
When Mrs. Bishop came to Denver j
she said she came from San Francisco
and that her home was in California, i
She was retiring and unepmmunicative, :
and those who made her acquaintance!
learned little of her affairs. She did not
make money here, and went to Colo
rado Springs in a search for a new lo
cation.
Mrs. Jones sent a letter to Colorado
Springs several days ago addressed to
Mrs. Bishop. It was returned, the
postal authorities having been unable
to locate the woman.
While the identification of the body
found as that of Mrs. Bouton is strong, ;
there is enough doubt about it to cause 1
much anxiety among Mrs. Bishop's j
friends.

posite views, based on the fact that

Speaker Prescott was located at the

be recognized and that we elect a Sen

friends for some time and I am thor
oughly familiar with his record. Men
tally, morally and socially he is all
that could be desired and would honor
California in the Senate of the United
States."

were under the impression that you
were pledged to Bard?" was asked.
"No, I was not," answered the
Speaker. "While it is true that during
the campaign the San Bernardino Sun
printed an article the headlines of
which said I was unequivocally pledged
to Bard, the article did not bear out
' this statement and explained my posi
tion correctly."
"Then you came here as a Flint man
and not as a Bard man, as was gener
ally supposed?"

cott. "I intended to vote for Mr. Flint

LEADERS ARE TO CONFER.
Meeting May Take Place in San Fran-
Cisco Next Saturday.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. 6.âThe first
conference of leaders to bring about a
union of the Republicans of the south
may take place in San Francisco next
Saturday or It may be postponed so
as'to take place here immediately after
the arrival of Senator Bard and U. i?.
Grant. The latter, it is said, has ar
ranged to attend the meeting of the
Presidential electors hi this city next
Monday. Events may suggest the in
troduction of Grant as a candidate ac
ceptable to the several factions.
The question is asked in all camps,
"Where will the Arthur Fisk bunch go
when the break comes?" The impres
sion gains that the supporters of the
Postmaster's candidacy will be used to
advance the position of some aspirant
from the south, so that Senator Per
kins shall not be confronted by obsta
cles four years hence.
The Ruef delegation of San Fran
cisco statesmen will disintegrate at the
"proper time." Knight boomers who
are claiming the ultimate support of
the San Francisco delegation will prob
ably find that Ruef has made other ar
rangements.
The notion that President Roosevelt
is losing much needed sleep nights in
consequence of the uncertainty of the
outcome of the battle for the Senator
ship in California is becoming stale and
unprofitable. It Is known that Roose
velt has a speaking acquaintance with
several of the candidates, and It is also
known in a general way that he doesn't
care which one succeeds provided the
Legislature does not choose a man who
is tied up hard and fast to the railroad
corporations.
The peculiarity of the present contest
in Sacramento Is the eagerness of as
pirants to convince the Legislature that
the railroad is with them in the fight
for the Senatorship, and also that they
will be with the President in his fight
on the railroads.

The definite announcement of Bard's
coming has naturally aroused great en
thusiasm at his headquarters and cre
ated excitement among those interest
ed in the election. The presence of a
real United States Senator as a can
didate will add largely to the import
ance of the battle.
Thomas Hughes of Los Angeles has
arrived to join the Bard forces, and his
j coming adds to the confidence in the
Senator's camp. Hughes is a leader of
recognized force in, the southern city,
and his enterprise and sagacity are
well known. His presence will add
much strength to the Bard fight. R.
N. Bulla, former Senator, is ex
pected here soon to aid in the Bard
fight, and other prominent Los An
geles Republicans may come up to as
sist. Bard does not lack powerful
friends in the city of angels.
Oxnard looks- upon himself as the
dark horseâas the man who must come
in to receive for the south that which
belongs to it in case neither Bard nor
Flint can reach the goal. He Is still
maintaining his headquarters and still
receives all coiners with that cheerful
smile and geniality that cannot but
make him friends, even if they are not
potent enough to draw votes already
tied up somewhere else.
L. M. Andrews and Walter Bacon
are still managing Oxnard's fight and
they show no dejection. It is evident
that they feel the lightning will tear
things up very close to them and con
sequently there is no talk of withdraw
ing the Oxnard camp. Oxnard points
to the fact that he is a friend of Presi
dent Roosevelt and that he is an advo
vate of the policy inaugurated by
Roosevelt and which will be carried
out for the next four years. It is
said by Oxnard's friends, however,
that his greatest desire is that the new
Senator shall be from the south and
that if he finds he cannot win the fight
he wil cast his influence in favor of
some other southern candidate.
A local paper, while Oxnard was in
San Francisco, published a story that
he had left Sacramento in a huff be
cause his candidacy had not been
taken seriously enough and that he
would not return. The publication
evidently perturbed Oxnard, who is
passing through his first campaign
and doesn't realize perhaps like some
of the other candidates just what a
soft path he is being allowed to travel.
So open and so quiet has been this
fight up to date that the candidates in
volved are drifting into a condition
that will cause them to die of shock if
they ever get mixed up in a real stren
uous battle for a toga.
*T merely went to San Francisco to I
see my brother off for the East," said I
Oxnard, "and transact some business. I
I haven't the least intention of going to |
China or anywhere else other than Sac-
I ramento for tne present.
One feature of Fisk's fight that is
I creating comment among the politi-
I cians is the fact that he is the first
[ Federal officeholder, in San Francisco
! who has made a fight for the "Senator
> ship while serving under the Govern
! ment. Others have come here to help
| candidates, but none to seek office
themselves. The late Collector, John P.
Jackson, interested himself in Perkins'
fight, United States Marshal Shine was
here for Grant in 1889, and John C.
Lynch, Internal Revenue Collector, was
here for Burns, later for Perkins and
now for Knight and Flint, but never
before has a man holding a Federal of
fice tried at the same time to become
United States Senator.
"Supposing Fisk should receive a tel
egram from the Postmaster General ad
vising him that his duties were in San
Francisco," suggested an Assembly
man, who, by the way, is not in the
Fisk column. He went no further than
the suggestion.
Fisk departed for the bay this after
noon, as did also John H. Lynch. There
will be a general exodus of candidates,
legislators and attaches to the me
tropolis to-morrow, and Sacramento
will relapse into its normal state until
Sunday night. Then they will come
surging back and the real life of the i
Senatoria4 contest will begin. Legis
lators who have been in doubt how
they were to cast their votes will have
made up their minds or received their
instructions: combinations will have
been decided upon. The last fierce
struggle to secure votes before the bal
loting begins will be inaugurated and !
the legislators will find themselves
pulled hither and thither by a whirl
wind of conflicting interests, the ma
jority to remain where they stood or
had been put, but some to waver and
change base, perhaps more than once
in the last two days before the ballot
ing should begin.
Knight and Flint are going down to
see Herrin and each will try to find out
whether he is to receive the votes that
the "Southen Pacific" controls. The
Ruef men at present are simply wait
ing.
Like soldiers on the field, who know
naught of what is before them, they
await the command that will send them
into the fray. "Somehow or another
they seem to think the man they land
on will be the Senator. They seem to
believe that other votes, controlled in
various ways, will go in the same di
rection. Incidentally some of them do
not think that $9 a day is enough
money for a man holding the dignified
title of Assemblyman. Ruef's men, i
some of them say, will never go for
Knight. The San Francisco boss dis
likes this particular Senatorial candi
date too much for that, they allege
It is said that Knight treated Ruef
with disdain at the Chicago convention,
and that the last named has treasured
this up.
Candidate Knight and Governor i
Pardee were to have had a talk to-day I
to renew felicitations and pledges of
friendship, but when the Senatorial
candidate called at the CaDitol the
Governor was deep in some matters of
legislation and Knight said he would
call another day. If he had had any
doubts on the matter, the publication
by The Call of the fact that the Gov
ernor had his hands entirely off the
Senatorial fight convinced Knieht that
the Governor was in no way obstruct
ing him.
Assemblymen from above Tehachapi

and one will undoubtedly be held by
the-northerners early next week if the!
south has not by that time united on a
man and elected him. The southern
delegation is holding many caucuses, i

DEADLY DUEL BETWEEN
OFFICERS AND A THUG
Capture of Pugilist's Brother Accom
plished After One Policeman Is
Slain and Another Wounded.
CHICAGO, Jan. 4.âOne person was
killed, one mortally wounded and two
were wounded less seriously to-day
during an attempt by the police to
capture Harry Feinberg, a brother of
former Pugilist "Kid" Farmer, now
in the penitentiary.
Policeman James Keefe and Rich
and Birmingham encountered Fein
berg to-day at Twenty-fifth place and
Wallace street. Without, warning
Feinberg fired twice at his would-be
captors. The policemen promptly re
turned the fire. During the fusillade
Birmingham was struck and slightly
wounded. Feinberg ran. entering a
saloon, the policemen following* In
the house three more shots were fired
by Feinberg, one lodging in Keefe's
abdomen. Keefe fell to the floor, dy
ing instantly. Saloon-keeper Frank
Gagan also was wounded.
In the excitement Feinberg escaped
from the house, but was captured
later, being trailed by blood in the
snow.

THEATER UNDER ARREST
Police Charge That He Locked Doors
and Exits of Playhouse During
a Performance.
LOS ANGELES, Jan: 5. âA special
from Pasadena says that warrants
were issued there to-day for the ar
rest of H. C. Wyatt, manager of the
Mason Opera-house of this city and
lessee of the Lowe Opera-house of
Pasadena, charging him with violat

conduct of theaters with reference to
the safety of the public. It is charged
that on December 31. the anniversary
cf the Iroquis Theater Are in Chicago,
the Chief of Police went to the Lowe
Opera-house, where a performance
was being given, and found all doors
and exits locked.
Father Accidentally Kills Son.
EVERETT, Wash., Jan. 5. âWhile
Splitting wood to-day Frederick Sop
per brained his 4-year-old son, John.
The child was swinging about a post
near where the man was working. The
little fellow's arm slipped and he fell
forward, striking face downward on
the chopping block just as the ax was
brought down with a heavy swing.
The blade sunk deep into the boy's skull
crosswise. He died three hours later.
The father is almost crazed with grief.

MARYSVILLE APPEAL
PASSES TO NEW HANDS
Colonel E. A. Forbes and His Political
Associates Now Control Tuba
I County's Pioneer Paper.
" The Marysville Appeal, one of tha
oldest daily papers in California, was
" sold yesterday to a Marysville com*
! | pany, at the head of which is Colonel
â¢j E. A. Forbes, the well known lawyer
iof that city. Fifteen days ago Forbes
' ; and a number of his associates pur
[ | chased the Mary sville Democrat and,
- I placing W. M. Cutter in the managing
, j editor's chair, declared that the future
i I policy of the paper would be political
.i iy independent.
. | Forbes and his political friends had
[ long been bitterly assailed by Editor
I I Frank Johnson of the Appeal and
i , when they got control of the Demo
! crat they declared war upon the rival
! newspaper owner. Realizing that
' ! Forbes and his associates, who are
i J prominent in Republican politics in
Yuba County, would be able to turn
the county patronage, which he had
enjoyed for many years, to their own
| paper. Editor Johnson gracefully de
clared his intention of retiring from the
newspaper field in that section of the
State. The result was the sale of the
Appeal to Forbes and his friends.
Colonel Forbes, who is staying at
the Palace Hotel, last night confirmed
the news from Marysville that the Ap
peal had been sold and added that
Railroad Commissioner A. C. Irwin,
j who is a director and stockholder in
~ the Democrat, had been appointed
temporary business manager of the
other paper. The Appeal will con
tinue as a Republican journal.

A Remedy Which Has Revolutionized

Troubles.

derful discovery nor yet a secret patent

anything except dyspepsia, indigestion
and stomach troubles with which nine
out of ten suffer.
The remedy is in the form of pleas
ant tasting tablets or lozenges, contain

'aseptic pepsin (Government test), gold
en seal and diastase. The tablets are
sold by druggists under the name of

teresting experiments to test the diges
tive power of Stuart's tablets show
that one grain of the active principle
contained in them is sufficient to thor
oughly digest 3000 grains of raw meat,
eggs and other wholesome food.
Stuart's Tablets do not act upon the
bowels like after dinner pills and cheap
cathartics, which simply irritate and
Inflame the intestines without having
any effect whatever in digesting food
or curing indigestion.
If the stomach can be rested and as
sisted in the work of digestion it will
very soon recover its normal vigor, as
no organ is so much abused and over- j
worked as the stomach.

crot, of the remarkable success of Stu- 1
art's Dyspepsia Tablets, a remedy
practically unknown a few- years ago !

This success has been secured entirely

Stuart's Dyspepsia Tablets act entire- !

pletely, so that it can be assimilated I
into blood, nerve and tissue. They cure !
dyspepsia, water brash, sour stomach, 1

they furnish the digestive power which '

ENJOINS CIVIL
SERVICE BOARD
Examinations for Ambulance
and Patrol Drivers Are
Stopped by Court's Order
The Civil Service Commission was
last evening served with an injunction,
issued by Judge Murasky, restraining
It from proceeding with the examina
tions for police patrol and ambulance
drivers. This is the third attempt on
the part of the commission during the
last two years to place civil service ap
pointees in these positions.
There are 627 applicants for the po
sitions of ambulance and patrol driv
ers. Last night 150 of them present
ed themselves at the commission's
rooms to take the physical examina
tion, and because of the restraining or
der they were turned away. .
City Attorney Long is prosecuting the
case. It is said that the present hold
ers of the positions in question were
instrumental in having the injunction
issued.
On November 22. 1902, the Civil Ser
vice Commission attempted to exert its
authority and place its appointees In
the positions in question. An injunc
tion was obtained by those holding the
positions. Another Injunction was is
sued March 21, 1903, the one yesterday
making the third.
The injunction was asked for on the
ground that the drivers should be class
ed as laborers and taken from the list
ol* laborers, the charter making no spe
cific provision for classifying drivers
under the civil service. If sustained
the injunction will leave the men now
employed as drivers still in their po
sitions.
GOVERNMENT TO STOP
THE SALE OF LIQUOR
Declares Against Drinking Resorts
Along the Line of the New
Irrigation Canals.
RENO, New, Jan. 5. âMembers of
the next General Assembly of this
State have Received word from the In
terior Department at Washington that
Nevada will be expected to take steps
ot the coming session of the Legisla
ture to stop the sale of liquor along
the line of the irrigation canals that
are being constructed in connection
with the reclamation work.
The Interior Department insists that
the various States that are enjoying
the benefits of the reclamation act
must give heed to the suggestion, as
the presence of liquor resorts along
tho line of the canals has resulted in
many crimes and seriously interfered
with the prosecution of the work.
The members of the Assembly living
at Reno are heartily in sympathy with
the department's suggestion and will
press the bill as soon as the Legisla
ture convenes.

OUR TREES
BACKBONE

President Makes a Stirring j

can Forestry Congress I
SPEAKS OF GREAT
DEMAND OF NATION
Wood Famine Will Be Inevi
table Result Says the Ex
ecutive, Unless Steps Are
Taken to Stop Destruction
! WASHINGTON, Jan. s.âPresident
1 Roosevelt was the principal speaker at
a special session of the American For
estry Congress held this afternoon at
, the National Theater. The President
accompanied by Secretary Loeb ar
i rived at the theater promptly at 3
j o'clock. He was escorted to the stage
! entrance, and as he appeared be*fore
' the immense audience which filled
every part of the house the Deople re
; ceived him standing, while the theater
rang with applause and the orchestra
played a patriotic air.
Secretary Wilson, in presenting
President Roosevelt, who was the first
speaker, made no address, simply in
troducing Mr. Roosevelt as the Presi
dent of the United States. After the
applause had subsided the President
spoke in part as follows, his address
i being punctuated frequently with ap-
I plause:
It is a pleasure to greet the members of the
i American Forestry Congress. You have made,
by your coming, a meeting which is without
parallel in the history of forestry. For the
first time the great business and the forest
, interests of the nation have joined together.
! through delegates altogether worthy of the or
, ganization they represent, to consider their in
: dividual and their common Interests in the
j forests.
The producers, the manufacturers and the
i great common carriers of the nation had long
I failed to realize their true and vital relation
to the great forests of the United States, and
forests and industries both suffered from the
failure. But the time of interference and mis
understanding has gone by. 1
j The place of the forest in the life of any
nation Is far too large to be described In the
rime at my command. This is particularly
true of its place in the United States. No
man Is a true lover of his country whose con
fidence In its progress and greatness is limited
to the period of his own life, and we cannot
afford for one instant to forget that our coun
try is only at the beginning of its growth. Un
less tho forests of the United States can be
made ready to meet the vast demands whlcn
this growth will inevitably bring, commercial
f disaster la inevitable.
I If the present rate of forest destruction is
' allowed to continue a timber famine is obvi
, ously inevitable. What such a famine would
mean to each of the industries of the t'nited
: States it is scarcely possible to imagine. And
the period of recovery from the injuries which
; a timber famine would entail would be meas
ured by the slow growth of the trees them-
I selves.
Whatever it may be possible for the Gov- '
I ernment to accomplish. Its work must ulti
-1 mately fall unless your interest and support
give it permanence and power. It is only as
j the producing and commercial interests of the
! country come to realize that they need' to have
! the trees growing up in the forest not less
l than they need the product of the trees cut
i down that we may hope to see the permanent
i prosperity of both safeiy-^eiureil.
This statement Is true* "not offfy as to for
j eats In private ownership, but as to the na- j
â¢ tional forests aa well. Unless the men from
j the West believe in forest preservation the :
. Western forests cannot be preserved. I am ]
heartily glad to know that Western sentiment
supports more and more vigorously the policy |
of setting aside national forests, the policy of j
creating a national forest service and espe
cially the policy of increasing the permanent
usefulness of these forest lands to all those
who come in contact with them.
At the conclusion of the address the
President, bowing his acknowledgments
i to the audience, left the theater and re
turned to the White House, the audi
ence standing and the band plavinsr
' "The Star-Spangled Banner" before he
! left the stage.
| Secretary Wilson then introduced M. !
, Jusserand. the Embassador from :
I France, who delivered an address on â¢
j "The Forest Policy of France."
Other speakers at the session were
I Howard Elliott, president of the North
!em Pacific Railroad, who spoke on
j "The Dependence of Business on the
Forest," and F. E. Weyerhauser of St. ]
! Paul.

SUPERIOR JUDGES
ELECT MULVEY AS
THEIR SECRETARY
POPULAR YOUNG MAN WHO HAS
BEEN CHOSEN SECRETARY OF
THE SUPERIOR COURT.
Thomas S. Mulvey was elected yes
terday as secretary of the Superior
Court Judges to fill the vacancy made
by the resignation of James W. Cof
froth. He received seven of the twelve
votes, the five others having been cast
for his only competitor, John F. Tyler,
assistant librarian of the law library.
On Wednesday, Judge Sloss being ab
sent. Mulvey received five votes and
Tyler six.
The Judges selected H. L. Joachim
son, former Police Judge, as Court
Commissioner to fill the vacancy made
by the promotion of Mulvey.
The new secretary is a young man,
extremely popular at the City Hall and
elsewhere, having made many friends
during his service as Court Commis
sioner. The Judges and other court
officials extended their congratulations.

NEW CHARGES
TO BE FILED
Dr. Irvine's Friends Will
Renew the Attack Upon
Bishop Ethelbert Talbot
â¢
FIRST ONSLAUGHT FAILS
Accusations Repudiated by
Certain of the Signers
Are Formally Withdrawn
PHILADELPHIA. Jan. s.âThe pre
senters of Bishop Ethelbert Talbot of
the Central Pennsylvania diocese of the
Protestant Episcopal Church, who have
not repudiated the paper filed against
him, to-night formally withdrew tht
charges in writing made against the
Bishop by friends of Dr. I. N. W. Ir
vine, the deposed priest. They reserve
the right to present new "charges in
writing."
The withdrawal is dated January 2.
and is addressed to Bishop Tuttle, to
whom a copy has been sent. The doc
ument begins by saying that all the
signers to the present paper knew the
contents and the purpose of the
"charges in writing" and enumerates
the changes as follows:
Crime, to wit: A criminal libel.
Immorality, to wit: False statements. i
Breach of ordination and consecration vowa.
Conduct unbecoming a Bishop.
Continuing, the document says:
We are in possession of such proof as is
sufficient, in our opinion, to put Bishop Talbot
upon trial. We believed, and sttll believe, that
the grounds are sufficient to put the accused
Bishop on his trial. The charges were formu
lated in order that the manifest scandal in
the church and the disgrace of the office of
Bishop Ethelbert Talbot should be inquired
into by the board of Inquiry, in the expectation
and belief that the board would direct the
church advocate to prepare a presentment to j
b<> signed by such of the board as agree
thereto. , I
Although Rev. Dr. Gilberson sisrned
the foregoing, he issued a statement to
night disavowing any further connec- |
tion with the case and will not allow j
his name to be used "to support such
fraud as has been perpetrated in this |
case." *
LINERS IN CLOSE RACE
ACROSS THE ATLANTIC
I I
Graf Waldersee and Cassel Arrive at
New York City Only Five j
Minutes Apart. ,
NEW YORK, Jan. 5. âArriving this
morning at Sandy Hook Aye minutes
apart the Graf Waldersee of the
Hamburg-American Line and the Cas
sel of the North German Lloyd Line
completed what was tantamount to a
3000-mile ocean race. From Dover
on the English Channel both had
steamed, bow and bow, the course
across the Atlantic and until Wednes
day were in plain sight of each other,
sometimes the one, sometimes the
other ahead and never more than Aye
minutes apart.
The officers of the two vessels deny
that there was any attempt to race,
tut none of the excitement of such a
contest was lacking. Captain Krech
of the Graf Waldersee ana Captain
Petermann of the Cassel both say that
never before in their sea experiences,
covering nearly forty years, have they
known two vessels to cross the At
lantic so near together.

LAW RECEIVES SENTENCE
Wealthy Chinese Sent to Jail for Try
ing to Import Countrymen Into
the United States.
CHICAGO, Jan. s.âConvicted by a
Jury In the United States District Court
on a charge of having attempted to
Import a Chinese into the United
States in violation* of the Chinese ex
clusion act, Lam Chee, a wealthy Chi
cago Chinese, was to-day sentenced to
two years in the House of Correction
and fined $1000.
Lam Chee was arrested after the
body of Leong Dick was found frosen
in a refrigerator car at SL Louis near- j
ly a year ago.

A CLOSE WATCH
Fears Thai an Attempt May
H<' Made to Lynch Alleged
Murderers ol Miner Dunn
ROBBERY THE MOTIVE
Report Is in Circulation at
Crescent City That Both
Suspects Have Confessed
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CRESCENT CITY, Jan. 5. â Harry
Brown ard Thomas Kelly, who are ac
cused of the murder of George Dunn,
ar. aged miner, in an isolated cabin on
the Crescent \.'ity and Grants Pass
road, were taken before Justice Bar
ry to-day, but on motion of the Prose
cuting Attorney their examination was
postponed, owing to the absence of
1 two important witnesses.
' It is reported to-night that the two
suspects have confessed to the Sheriff
and Prosecuting Attorney and that
they will waive examination. Feeling
is running high in this vicinity, as
j Dunn was well liked by his neighbors,
I and a close guard is being kept over
j the prisoners. It it feared by some
: that a lynching may follow if it is
i true that the men have confessed to
: the terrible crime. Dunn was beaten to
death in his lonely abode by robbers,
who- secured only a few dollars from
â  him.
GIVES UP HER EFFORT
TO BREAK A WILL
San Francisco Woman Enters Into
Compromise With Residuary Leg
atee, a Catholic Priest.
NASHUA, N. H., Jan. 6.âMrs.
McCabe of San Francisco, who, on
behalf of the heirs of the late Miss
Mary Burps of this city, figured as
contestant of the will of, Miss Burns,
settled the case out of court to-day.
Rev. P. J. O'Donnell was the pro
ponent, and it is understood that the
suit brought last fall has been settled
to the satisfaction of both parties.
Miss Burns left a very large property,
mostly real estate, in this city, and
Father O'Donnell was named as ex
ecutor and residuary legatee.

Ts Badly Injured by Negro
in Her Home in Louisville
BLOODHOUNDS ON TRAIL
Citizens of Blue Grass City
Aroused to Great Excite
ment by Act of African
LOUISVILLE, Ky., Jan. s.âMiss
Alice Brightwell, a protege in the
family of John T. Bate, a bank teller,
was attacked to-day by a negro in
Bate's residence at Crescent Hill, a
high-class residence portion of Louis
ville. The young woman was badly
injured.
A detailed force was at once sent
to the scene and four officers with ;
bloodhounds have taken up the trail
of the negro. The Crescent Hill dls- I
trlct is in a state of high excitement
and Captain Reilley of the Louisville j
police has given orders if the negro be ;
found he be brought to the County Jail
here Instead of being taken to the po
lice station.
URGES ESTABLISHMENT
OF CHILDREN'S PLAYGROUND
School Board Will Ask Supervisors to
Devote Part of Block in Mis
sion to Purpose.
The Board of Education yesterday
received a communication from Su
perintendent Joseph Myers suggesting
that a portion of the block opposite
the Mission High School be set apart
for children's playground purposes in
view of the fact that the city is about
to acquire Mission blocks 86 and 87 '
for park purposes. The board thought
the suggestion a good one and dele
gated Director Altmann to confer with
the Board of Supervisors relative to
the improvement.
Teachers' certificates were granted
to F. J. Browne, Charles S. Downes,
Tracy R. Keliey and Misses L. McDer
mott, Isabella Evans, Frances Cook,
Belle Smith and Mary Busteed.
The petition of the Thirty-ninth '
District Improvement Club that a
portable schoolhouse be placed in
West Richmond District and that bet
ter accommodations be granted for the
Park Primary School was taken un- \
advisement.
Leaves of absence were granted to
Misses F. M. Bliven, Madge Sprott,
Sophia Kraus. Edith Boyle and Mrs.
F. M. Grosjean, teachers in the de- j
pprtment.
The board adopted the Gallagher-
Marsh system for the study of stenog- .
raphy in the schools.

WITH HIGH CEREMONIES
Officers of Golden Gate Aerie Are For
mally Inducted in Their New
Positions.
The public installation of the of-
Beers for the ensuing year of Golden
Gate Aerie No. 61, Fraternal Order of
Eagles, was attended with high cere
monies at Native Sons' Hall last night.
Nearly 1000 people were present to
witness the installation. The hall was
profusely decorated with American |
RagS and the balcony was hung with
red, white and blue bunting. Worthy i
Past Grand President Harry Frolich
presided.

priate music. All the officers were in <
the full dress uniform of the order.

Dancing wound up the festivities.

RECEIVER OF SHIPYARD
TRUST ASKS $200,000
James Smith Jr. Wants Fee. Which
Reorganization Company Says
Is Exorbitant.
â¢ NEWARK, N. J., Jan. 5.âA fee of
$200,000 for James Smith Jr. for act
ing as receiver for the United States
Shipbuilding Company was asked of
Judge Lanning in the United States
District Court Wednesday. Counsel
for the Sheldon reorganization com
mittee opposed the application and
said that the sum named was exorbit
ant. It was finally agreed to refer the
Question to a master to take testimony.

CALIFORNIA INCREASES
HER GOLD PRODUCTION
Figures Submitted to Mint Director
Show Largest Output In
Many Years.
WASHINGTON. Jan. s.âThe prelim
inary figures upon the production of
gold and silver in the United States in
1904 submitted to the Director of the
Mint show larger gains over the pre
ceding year than were expected. Near
ly every State of importance in yield
has increased its output. California
has made, the best output for many
years, as follows: Gold, $19,000,000; sil
ver, $1,380,000.
Among the notable increases in the
silver production over 1903 are: Cali
fornia, 460,000 fine ounces, and Idaho,
493,000 ounces. Colorado shows a loss
of about 490,000 ounces; Nevada, a loss
of about 550,000 otmces and Utah 676,000
ounces.
In the production of gold Alaska
shows a gain of about $386,000; Califor
nia, $2,895,500; Colorado. $3,460,000; Ida
ho $390,000: Montana. $549,000; Nevada.
$1,572,000; South Dakota, $443,000, and
Utah, $1,000,000.
MONEY IN STATE TREASURY
MAY BE LOANED TO BANKS
Attorney General Webb Renders an
Opinion on the Subject to
Governor Pardee.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. s.âAttorney
General Webb has rerfdered an opin
ion to Governor Pardee anent the sug
gestion of Controller Colgan that the
money in the State treasury be loaned
to banks that are willing to pay inter
est and furnish bonds as security.
Webb advises that an act be passed
by the Legislature authorizing the
loaning of the money and points out
the fact that the views of the Su
preme Court as to the constitutionality
of the bill could then he obtained.

ON TRIAL FOR PERJURY
IN IOWA MURDER CASE
Woman Accused of Testifying Falsely
in Connection With Her Hus
band's Death.
MARENGO, la., Jan. 6.âThe second
trial of Mrs. Sarah Ellen Gallaugher,
on the charge of perjury in the trial
for the murder of her husband on
June 19, 1902, has commenced here
before Judge Byington, the defense
having secured a change of venue from
Johnson County.
James Gallaugher was murdered
while he lay Bleeping. Footprints
leading to the home of Charles Hallada
were found and the latter was placed
under arrest. He was convicted of
complicity in the murder and given a
life sentence. Mrs. Gallaugher was
also indicted, testified in her own be
half and was acquitted. She was after
ward indicted for perjury, but the ver
dict was set aside by the Supreme
Court.

JURY IS IMPANELED
TO TRY SMELTER SUIT
Shasta County Fruit Growers Say
That Fumes From Roaster De
stroy Their Crops.
REDDING. Jan. 6.âA Jury was se
cured Wednesday and the taking of tes
timony commenced In the suit brought
by eight farmers and fruit growers
against the Mountain Copper Com
pany to recover $34,500 damages al
leged to have been done their crops
and trees by fumes from the smelt
ing plant at Keswick. Some of the
fruit growers live seventeen miles
from Keswick and claim that the
fumes from the Keswick roasters pre
vent their trees bearing.
There is to be expert testimony on
the part of the company tending to
show that roaster fumes are beneficial
to trees rather than detrimental.

A Rare Spot.
One of the queerest corners of the
earth Is Chatham Island off the coast
of Ecuador. This island lies 600 miles
west of Guayaquil, and the equator
runs directly through it. Captain Rein
man, who was sent to the Galapagos
group of islands to inquire into the
proper grounding of a deep-sea cable,
stopped at Chatham Island, and says
it abounds in cats, every one of which
is black. These animals live in the
crevices of the lava foundation near
the coast, and subsist by catching fish
and crabs instead of rats and mice.
Other animals found on this island are
horses, cattle, dogs, goats and chickens,
all of which are perfectly wild.âlndian
apolis News.
There does not seem to be much
"graft" In Australia. All its public
men who have died in recent years
died poor. The late Sir George Dibbs.
whose will was probated recently, left
only about $10,000.

NEWS OF THE COUNTY OF ALAMEDA
OLNEY'S ORDER
IN OPERATION
'â¢' Street Department Forces
Rip Out Many Signs That
. Had Not Been Removed
CLEAN IP IS GENERAL
Mayor Hopes the Next Step
Will Be to Bury All Elec
trical Wires Underground
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 5.
Street Inspector W. W. Blair and Su
perintendent of Streets C. F. Ott with
a force of men armed with sawst picks,
hammers and tongs and guarded by j
two policemen ripped, sawed, cut and I
dug standing signboards, posts and,
other sidewalk obstructions to-day
â long' Broadway. So far as the Street j
Department force went they cleaned the â¢
sidewalks thoroughly. The work will
be continued throughout the city.
The crusade was conducted in strict
adherence to the Mayor's instructions.
Merchants were given twenty-four
hours' notice to remove , their street
signs. Those who did not heed the
word were visited as rapidly as the J
street gang could move. Many oid
signs were carted off to the corpora
tion yard, their owners preferring to
permit the city to remove them instead
of going to the expense themselves. The
work was accomplished without serious
objections. Here and there a shopkeep
er rebelled, but by dint of tactful ex
planation Inspector Blair persuaded
the objecting one to join the procession.
Such signs as were taken in charge
by the city were those that had long
ago passed the stage of usefulness. It
is not the intention of the city officials
to cause unnecessary annoyance to
business men who have unwittingly
gone to heavy expense in installing
electrical displays. Reasonable- time
will be given for reconstruction of such
aigns so that they shall not obstruct
the streets or sidewalks.
Electric signs or others that are fasti
to buildings and can be, swung flush
with the property line when not in use i
will not be disturbed. There are a num- 1
ber of signs which are set fast to posts
and project across the sidewalk. These j
have been ordered down. Mayor Olney i
said to-day after Inspecting had
been accomplished in the direction of
improvement that he was in favor of
continuing the movement until all side
walk obstructions had been removed.
The next step will be toward ridding
the business section as rapidly as can
be done of all telegraph and telephone
poles.
All of the mhnicipal fire alarm and
'police telegraph wires are above
ground. Superintendent George Bab
cock estimates the cost of putting them
underground at $50,000. In his annual
report to the Board of Police and Fire
Commissioners Chief Engineer N. A.
Ball of the Fire Department has made
a, strong recommendation that all over
head- wires be removed as soon as pos
" sjble.
Many hundreds of telephone wires j
have been placed in underground con- !
. duits. The telephone company has also
Installed underground pipes for use in j
connecting lines with Its new buildings
on Franklin street, near Fifteenth.

SUGAR BEETS
NEED THE SUN
Chemistry Division of the
Department of Agriculture
Tells of Its Research
WASHINGTON, Jan. s.âThat high
temperature and sunshine are the
dominant factors in producing the best
quality of sugar beets is anounced as
the result of five years' experiments
which have just been concluded by the
chemistry division of the Department
of Agriculture. The data obtained is
expected by agricultural officials to
save Immense amounts to capital by
pointing out on what sugar beet in
dustries should be instituted.
The experiments were made in lo
calities ranging from New York to
North Carolina and entirely across the
continent. The environments the in
fluence of which sugar beets require,
the soil, cultivation and the period of
artificial fertilization, temperature,
hours of sunshine and of cloudiness,
elevation and latitude -and longitude
are pointed out. The results indicate
that a high temperature is the greatest
of these factors in the producing of
beets with sunshine next in impor
tance.
The average temperature of the lo
calities, according to these results, not
exceeding 70 degrees Fahrenheit during
the three growing months, June, July
and August, is most desirable. Above
this figure richness of the beets con
stantly diminishes, and the longer day
and consequently longer hours of sun
shine makes a location best fitted for
high sugared beets.
TWO ARID LAND BILLS.
Senntor Bard Presents Irrigation Com
mittee Measures.
WASHINGTON, Jan. 5. â Senator
Bard, chairman of the Committee on
Irrigation, to-day introduced two bills
directed toward the development of
arid lands. One authorizes the Secre
tary of the Interior to construct Irriga
tion works along the shores of Little
Kiamath, Lave, Tule, Rhett and
Goose lakes, which lie on the boun
dary between Oregon and California.
The other provides that all lands cedefp
or purchased under the provisions of
the reclamation act shall be dis
posed of according to the same; that
lands not hitherto surveyed may be
surveyed and formed into farm units
of ten acres and that the Secretary of
the Interior may lease the lands for
such time as they are not needed in
connection with the reclamation plans.
The Senate Committee on Com
merce has reported Perkins' bill pro
viding for a steam tender for the en
gineer service of the Twelfth Light
house District, to cost $135,000. '

FRIEND TO GET PLACE
Bullock Can Have the United States

WASHINGTON, Jan. s.âCaptain

undoubtedly will be appointed United

ka. There are dozens of applicants
for the place, but Captain Seth can
have it if he wants it. He is now

ers, in which country he has spent
most of his life.
Bullock was marshal of Deadwood
in the early days and was the first
man to establish order there.
Among the applicants for the place
are J. P. ClÂ»m, postofflce inspector in
charge at New York, and Timothy
of San Francisco, and Mike
Sullivan of New York, both of whom
are mining in Alaska.
NATURALIZATION OF ALIENS.
I Secretary Hay's Recommendations
Laid Before Congress.
WASHINGTON. Jan. 5. âSecretary
I Hay's recommendations on the na
turalization of afiens, laid before Con
gress, are that the law should restrict
naturalization to certain courts defi
nitely named and forbid all other
courts from entertaining naturaliza
tion proceedings; that the nature of
the testimony upon which naturaliza
tion may be conferred should be more
particularly prescribed by law than
it is now; th" 4 the language and
form of certificates should be pre
scribed; that they should be printed
upon a particular paper, made for the
purpose, so as to prevent the extensive
counterfeiting which now prevails,
and that all courts having power to
naturalize'should be. required to make
returns of naturalization to ihe gen
eral Government.

passerby what was in their wagon.
