ination by the President
WASHINGTON, Jan. s.âThe Senate
Committee on Commerce to-day au
thorized a favorable report on the nom
ination of William B. Crum to be Col
lector of the Port of Charleston. S. C.
Confirmation of the Crum nomination
has been opposed for three sessions of
Congress by Senator Tillman, on the
ground that "a colored man is oblec
tionable to many of those who conduct
business through the Charleston cus
tom-house."
Crum is filling the position of Col
lector on a third recess appointment,
and the indications are that he will
now be confirmed by the Senate, de
spite the .objections of Senator Till
man.
MINORITY MARINE REPORT.
Discriminating Dnty Flavored Instead
of Direct Government Aid.
WASHINGTON. Jan. s.âThe views
of the minority of the Merchant Ma
rine Commission have been prepared
in the form of a report, which to-mor
row will be. made iif the Senate by
Senator Mallory of Florida, and in
the House of Representatives by Rep
resentative Spight of Mississippi. Sen
ator Martin of Virginia is the only
Democratic member of the commis
sion who has not subscribed to the
minority views.
Representative Spight is the author
of the minority report, the principal
feature of which is advocacy of the
discriminating duty policy as a sub
stitute for direct Government aid for
American ship-owners, as proposed by
the bill presented by the commission.
In this bill the minority says: "We
cannot help but see the 'trail of tiie
serpent' that has been over all other
bills on this subject."
Army Orders.
WASHINGTON, Jan. s.âBy order of
the War Department First Lieutenant
John D. Yost, assistant surgeon, is re
lieved from duty in San Francisco and
will proceed on the transport to sail
about February 1 to Honolulu to relieve
Major William B. Davi3, surgeon. Major
Davis will proceed to Manila for duty.
Captain Robert S. Woodson, assistant
surgeon, is relieved from duty at Fort
Clark, Texas, and will proceed to Fort
McDowell, California, for duty. First
Lieutenant Charles W. Farr, assistant
surgeon, in addition to his present du
ties will perform the duty of attending
surgeon in San Francisco. Corporal
Herbert C. Wesley, band, Twenty-first
Infantry, Presidio, is transferred to the
Twenty-eighth Infantry as private. He
will be sent to the headquarters of that
regiment at Fort Snelling, Minnesota.
Major Walter A. Bethel is relieved from
duty as judge advocate, Columbia, and
will proceed to Manila for duty as
judge advocate in the Department of
Luzon to relieve Major Frank L. Dodds,
who will proceed, to San Francisco and
report by telegraph to the military Sec
retary. First-class Private Moses Mont
gomery, hospital corps, now at Steelton,
Pa., upon expiration of the furlough
granted him from the Presidio, Mon
terey, will report to the commanding
officer at Fort Niobrara, Nebraska. Re
cruit Charles E. White, cavalry, Jeffer
son Barracks, Missouri, is transferred
to the signal corps. He will be sent to
Benlcia Barracks. Contract Surgeon A.
M. Wilkins is relieved from duty at
Fort McDowell. California, and will
proceed to his home at Delta, Ohio, and
report by letter to the surgeon general
of the army for annulment of contract.

RENOWN OF BUENCAMINO
Notable Filipino. Whose
Boy Is at Berkeley, Sub
ject of Elaborate Story
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street, Jan. 5.
j The eyes of Felipe Buencamlno, a
j Filipino student at the State Univer
. sity, bulged out with delight to-day,
I when he was shown copies of the Cen
tury magazine, containing an illus
trated article which described in eu
logistic fashion young Buencamino's
father, who is one of the most promi
nent Filipinos on the islands.
David Gray, a correspondent and an
army officer . from . America spent
i Christmas with the Buencaminos in
! Manila, and Gray has written of the
! family, and the Christmas celebration
j enjoyed with its members.
I The son, who is at the university, is
'.referred to in the article, in connection
| with a paragraph which describes the
J senior Buencamino's habits of modern
| thought.
â¢ Pictures of the elder man, of his
wife and daughters adorn the article,
and the youngster at the university
danced with delight as he recognized
the loved familiar faces, in scenes
which to him are home.
The elder Buencamfno is rated as one
of the most able of the Filipinos. For
his services to the American Govern
ment he has been rewarded with power
and position. His loyalty to the Fili
pino people was not questioned during
their resistance to both Spain and
America, but when wisdom and
patriotism (these being the words of
Buencamino's admirers) dictated that
the Filipino people could serve their
own interests better by co-operation
with Americans than by resisting
them, then Buencamino "came in," as
the saying is. and with him most of the
influential men .of his party.
Aguinaldo is a friend and intimate
of the Buencaminos. Young Buencam
ino, at the university, knows the fa
mous war chief of the Filipinos from
having seen him at the Buencamino
household. The oÂ§ce belligerent war
rior is now at peace, but though de
feated, Buencamino says he has not
lost his hold on the Filipinos, who still
revere him.
SEEKS RELIEF IN DEATH
FROM BURDEN OF WOES
Alexander Main, an Aged and Lonely
Scotsman, Finds Life Too
Heavy to Endure.
OAKLAND, Jan. b. â Despondent
over the death of â  his wife, ill and
troubled about his son's burdens, Alex
ander F. Main, 77 years old, ended his
earthly woes to-night by shooting
himself In the head while alone in the
old Horace Carpentler residence, 252
Third street.
Neighbors heard a shot shortly after
6 o'clock this evening and summoned
Patrolman Kyle, who found the body
in the room, the pistol lying near by.
Friends of the old man said he had
been brooding for a long time over his
condition. Soon after Mrs. Main's
death several months ago Herbert
Main, the son, an employe of the
Southern Pacific Company, met an ac
cident which cost him many weeks of
suffering in the Railroad Hospital and
the loss of several fingers. Young
Main was barely out of bed when his
wife was taken seriously ill from ty
phoid fever and was sent to the hos
pital. These things distressed the
elder Main greatly.
Main was a native of Scotland. For
many years he was in business as an
expert accountant. Coroner Mehr
mann took charge of the body.
VAN HORNS WILL FIGHT
OVER POSSESSION OF BOY
Attorney for Mother Believes Appeal
From Divorce Decree Stays
Giving Son to Father.
OAKLAND, Jan. s.âWillis Van
Horn, the 14-year-old son of Professor
Ross Van Horn and Marion Van Horn
of Berkeley, was not produced in court
this morning by the mother, as di
rected by a writ of habeas corpus left
for her at her home at 2222 Bancroft
way, Berkeley, last night. This after
noon, however, Mrs. E. Sloane, a sis
ter bf Mrs. Van Horn's, appeared and
stated that she was not at home and
upon representations of her attorney
the matter was continued until Mon
day.
When the mother did not appear
this morning Van Horn felt fully con
vinced that the boy had been taken
out of the Jurisdiction of the court,
but the appearance of the sister and
her attorney allayed his anxiety. He
was granted the custody of the child
when given a divorce some weeks ago,
but has been unable to get the child
and has Instituted these proceedings,
to have it given him through the
courts. Attorney W. W. McNair, for
Mrs. Van Horn, has filed notice of an
appeal in the divorce case and is in
hopes that this will act as a stay on
the court's order until the appeal has
been heard.
Finds New Double Stars.
BERKELEY, Jan. 5. âMore double
stars have been discovered and meas
ured at the Lick Observatory. The
latest bulletin issued by the University 1
Press contains an account of another
hundred new double stars discovered
and measured by Assistant Astronomer
R. G. Aitken. These new double stars
are of the same character as those pre- I
vlously discovered at the Lick Obser
vatory. The distance between the two !
components in most of them is very I
small and nearly all would be difficult
objects to observe under conditions less
favorable than obtain at Mount Ham
ilton.
Special attention is called to two of
these new' pairs, because they consist
of closer components than previously
known double stars and are likely to
prove interesting binary systems.

OLD ST. JOHN'S
HALF CENTURY
Mother Episcopal Church of
Alameda County to Cele
brate Its Golden Jubilee
THREE DAYS' SERVICES
Members to Hold Memorial
in Honor of Father Aker
ly, Founder of the Parish
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 6.
The celebration of the Jubilee anni
versary of organization of St. John's j
Episcopal Church of Oakland will be- ,
gin at 10 o'clock to-morrow morning
with the parochial celebration of holy
communion and will continue until j
Sunday evening. January 8. Special
services will be held on each of the
three days, including a union service of
all the parishes of Alameda County,
and a memorial service for the Rev.
Benjamin Akerly, D. D., the founder of
Bt. John's Church. The Right Rev.
William Ford Nichols, Bishop of the di- j
ocese; the Right Rev. Sidney C. Par
tridge, Bishop of Kioto, Japan; the
Rev. W. A. Brewer, registrar of the
diocese; the Rev. David McClure of
San Francisco, the Rev. S. J. Lee and
the rectors of the Episcopal churches
of Alameda County will participate in
tho jubilee celebration.
St. John's Episcopal Church was or
ganized in 1855 and is known as the
"mother church" of Alameda County.
The church was established by the
Rev. Benjamin Akerly, D. D., who for
many years was its rector. The first
regular service was held on the first
Sunday after Epiphany, in 1855. in an
upper room on the corner of Broadway
and Seventh street. During the long
rectorship of Dr. Akerly, while St.
John's was yet a mission, its clergy
man and people reached out to plant
new altars in other places, and to as
sist in maintaining those already set
up. To read the history of St. John's
Church is to read a very important
part of the history of the other Epis
copal churches in Alameda County.
On Sunday morning, December 13,
1896, the main building of the church
was destroyed by fire, but the guild
room and the vestry were saved, and
with them were saved the parish rec
ords, containing all the more important
historical records of the church. The
present church building at Eighth and
Grove streets was erected almost on
the site of the >one destroyed by fire,
and the Rev. W. A. Brewer, who will
preach the jubilee sermon next Sunday
evening, preached the sermon at the
dedication of the new edifice.

FAMILY OF SIX
CROSSES DESERT
W. A. Wright, Wife and Four
Daughters Travel From
Colorado in an Old Wagon
ARRIVE IN BERKELEY
Five Teams of Horses Worn
Out in Long and Tedious
Journey to California
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call.
2148 Center Street, Jan. 5.
The days of '49 were recalled to
those who have read of tbe overland
pilgrimages made at that time when
Warren A. Wright, hla wife
and four daughters rolled into
Berkeley last night in a "P r & iri Â®
schooner," in which they have lived
since July 7. The girls and their par
ents have traveled 5:400 miles over
desert, mountain, river and P- laln
wearing out five pairs of horses in tne
journey, and at last they have found
a haver- in South Berkeley.
The father was in search of employ
ment when he left Boulder. Colo., si*
months ago. He is a paper-hanger.
Fayette, Idaho, was his objective point.
That was a 400-mile trip. There was
nothing for him at Fayette, and then
began the long overland Journey to
California, through the Northwest and
down the coast by way of Oregon.
Mrs. Wright said to-day:
The trip wae a weird one. I wonder we lived
through it. Once when our horses gave out on
the desert nobody would sell ue hay. Some
times the alkali and thirst nearly drove us
wild. At night we often fought mosquitoes,
unable to sleep. Often we helped the animals
pull the wagon through the sand and snow.
At night we would think of home and pray.
The four girls of the family range in
age from 16 to 4 years. The Wrights
have rented a cottage at Fifty-eighth
and McCalla streets. When they light
ed the fires there last night it was the
first inclosure they had found shelter
In, besides their tented wagon, since
July 7.
OAKLAND'S MOST
GIFTED SINGER
NOW A BRIDE
BY ZOE GREEN RADCLIFFE.
OAKLAND. Jan. announce
ment of the marriage last evening of
Miss Carolyn Crew to E. Walter Kasor
is one of the many surprises the new
year has had in store for us. It is only
lately that the friends of the talent
ed young singer have begun to suspect
thatf mere man was sharing her devo
iior( to art, and preparations for last
night's event were so quietly made
that few knew it was to be.
This wedding was a charming home
affair, a profusion of Christmas berries,
and holiday greens supplying an ar
tistic setting for the nuptial picture.
The ceremony was simplicity itself.
Rev. Dr. Dille officiating. There were
no attendants, the bride being given
into her husband's keeping by her
father, J. S. Crew.
The bride, who is petite and bru
nette, wore neither veil nor bridal
white, her gown being an exquisite cre
ation of pearl gray chiffon. A notice
able ornament glittered on her breast â
the diamond cross, which was the wed
ding gift of her mother.
Tnis happy young bride of a day
has probably the most perfect lyric
soprano of any singer in California.
Her voice acquired a rare perfection
of tone under the careful training of
Clement P. Rowlands, who. up to his
recent departure for England, was her
only tutor. Siuce then she has been
studying with Dr. H. J. Stewart of
San Francisco.
Mrs. Ra3or avers that marriage shall
make no change in the musical career
she has mapped out for herself. She
will continue to occupy her position
as leading soprano in the choir of the
First Methodist Church, and will also
take part, as planned, in the opera
Miss Cope is now rehearsing for the
big Fabiola benefit.
Mr. and Mrs. Rasor will enjoy their
honeymoon in the southern part of
the State, and on their return will re -
side at 1376 Haight street, San Fran
cisco, where the groom has made ready
a beautiful home for his bride.
The groom is vice president of the
well-known lumber firm of Sudden &
Christenson.

ATTACK OF HYDROPHOBIA
Patient En Route to Pasteur Institute
Succumbs on Board Brooklyn
Perry-Boa t.
NEW YORK, Jan. 6. âWnils on the
way to a Pasteur institute in Brook
lyn, accompanied by a physician,
Henry L*nehan of Wilkeabarre. Pa.,
said to be tha son of a well-known
attorney, died in great agony from
hydrophobia. He was bitten two
months ago by a pet spaniel, and, al
though little indication of rabies de
veloped, it was decided to bring him
here for treatment.
According to the physician who ac
companied him, the moment
heard the swashing of the waves
against the ferry-boat in the river he
went into a spasm. The doctor lmme
dlately applied drugs and revived Mm,
but before the boat reached the ferry
slip in Brooklyn the unfortunate
young man was attacked a secona
t 'ln the ferry-house he was overcome
again, barking and shrieking in a man
ner to frighten the crowd. Despite the
best efforts of the doctor he died In a
few minutes.

DETECTIVE FOUND DEAD
IN CHAIR IN APARTMENTS
Police of Chicago Believe official s
Death Was Accomplished by
Foul Means.
CHICAGO, Jan. 5. âIn the death of
George H. Venekamp, a detective, the
police believe they have a possible
murder mystery. A week ago Vene
kamp was beaten Into insensibility by
two men who attacked him on the
street and it was only two days ago
that he left a hospital. His body was
found upright in a chair in his apart
ments. There were bruises on the
head, but the police could not deter

EXPECT LIVELY
TIME IN DENVER
Legislature of Colorado
Is Keady to "Do Things*'
at Its Session To-Day
DENVER, Jan. s.âTo-morrow the
Colorado Legislature will "commence
to do things." When the joint session
of the House and Senate was dissolved
at 5:30 o'clock this afternoon all the
preliminaries to the canvassing of the
vote for State officers had been swept
away.
It is known that Lieutenant Gover
nor Haggott is of the opinion that the
courts are the proper place for the ad
justment of political contests, and an
effort may be made to induce or force
him to give way to Speaker Dickson,
who is favored by those members of
the Legislature who believe that the
contest between the rival candidates
for the Governorship should be settled
by the Legislature itself.
The message of Governor Peabody
read by the Governor, dealt, for the
most part, with recommendations con
corning reports frcm the various Slate
institutions. On the subject of the
militia, he touched briefly, referring the
Legislature to the executive report of
Adjutant General Sherman Bel, which
was so large that the Governor de
clared that he could not attempt to
epitbmize it. On the question of elec
tions, the message was as follows:
The fraudulent conduct oi elections under
our present system demands your immediate
and conc-rten action. in a general way it
seems sufficient to say that a modern primary
and registration law should be enacted and
the advisability of introducing voting machines
should be considered. In sorrow we must ad
mit that (ha elective franchise, especially in
Denver, has been sadly misused and prosti
tuted. A patient people protest and these out
raiti- should Us ended.
On the labor troubles the Governor,
after urging the adoption of a reason
able eight-hour law, said:
B*r!y In ;ny administration a certain organ
ization hnown as the Western Federation of
Miners, claiming public consideration under
the nr-me of labor, whose officers and those In
direct charge of Its management are bold,
careless, reckless men. attempted to ferment
tuMc in several industrial sections cf Ctol
i rado, to the end that that particular organi
sation should have recognition In the opera
ar.d manasfrnent of the mines, mills and
j-melteis, wherever located In the State, which
Â»rr.:ts culminated in the arbitrary calling of
the most senseless, causeless, unjustifiable and
inexcusable strikes ever known in this or any
otter country.
I recommend the passage o* an act by your
honorable body prohibiting boycotting, picket
ing, unfair lists or other Interference with the
lawful business or occupation of others and
ptwvtde a suitable penalty therefor.
Both Democrats and Bepublicans in
sist that everything will pass off peace
ably. The sole prophet to the con
trary was Representative Bromley.
who with comic seriousness'objected to
a railing being placed across two of the
three archways in the rear of the
House. Representative Freeman pro
posed the railing with the idea of pro
tecting the members in the rear seats
from the crowding by spectators. Rep
resentative Bromley, who sits in the
last row, declared that he wished no
railing behind his seat.
"There may be things doing in this
room before long," he asserted, "and I
may want to get out quick."
The roar of laughter that followed
his words effectually disposed of the
proposition to build the railing.

cific stock which they turned over to

which was valued at $75,098.000.

rari from tho United States Supreme
Court will act as a stay and prevent

OPPOSES DISSOLUTION
OF IRRIGATION DISTRICT
Attorney of Escondido Says Petition
Was Not by Majority of
Stockholders.
SAN DIEGO. Jan. 6. âAttorney F.
P. Willard of Escondido this ' after
noon filed a document in the Superior
Court opposing the dissolution of the
Escondido Irrigation District. It is al
leged that the petition for the dissolu
tion of the district was not signed by
a majority of the land owners of the
district and that the assessment made
is not a valid lien against the proper
ty. It is further alleged that the plan
proposed is a scheme to change the
irrigation district into a stock com
pany and sever the water from the
land.
Some weeks ago the property own
ers of the district voted to pay off
the indebtedness of the district
amounting to a quarter of a million
dollars. This wjas done and it was
then proposed to form a water asso
ciation. Attorney Willard says the as
sociation would not be a public utility,
but on the contrary would'be a close
corporation. The court has not yet
confirmed the dissolution. The pro
test will be heard by Judge Conklin
at a date not yet set.

ROAD INTO THE BIG BASIN

County Appropriates $12,000 to

BOULDER CREEK. Jan. 5. âThe
Board of Supervisors of Santa Cruz
County has appropriated $12,000 to
construct a wagon road from Boulder
Creek to California Redwood Park,
commonly known as the Big Basin.
Work will commence at once and it
is believed the road will be open for
travel by summer. The route adopted

all dangerous places.

CESSION OF THE YOSEMITE.
MEMBERS of the Native Sons' organization originated the
proposition to re-cede the Yosemite Valley and the Mari
posa Big Tree Grove to the Federal Government. The val
ley is now surrounded by the Yosemite Xatibnal Park, which is
under Federal jurisdiction, and the Big Tree Grove is surrounded
by a forest reserve, also under Federal jurisdiction.
The originators of the re-cession policy sought its indorsement
by the State Board of Trade. That body is in such high repute as
the promoter of all the material interests of the State that its in
dorsement is continually solicited by persons and organizations for
matters of public concern. The power and position of the State
Board of Trade have been preserved by the caution with which it
examines all these appeals. W hen any matter gets its indorsement it
is passed to the confidence of the State. The State Board refused
to indorse the policy of re-cession of the Yosemite Valley and Big
Tree Grove, and immediately provided a plebiscite to test the con
dition of public opinion as to that matter, for the benefit of the
Legislature.
This wise course on the part of the board is justified by the
general attention that has been aroused. So far it is apparent that
the proposition does not meet with favor. The reasons given for the
opposition are various, but in the mass of expressions appears some
influence looking to the use of the opinions presented as an influence
upon the Legislature, to secure large appropriations for the valley
from the State treasury.
This makes proper a statement of the reasons put forward by
the proponents of the re-cession, since these seem to have received
no consideration by a majority of those who have expressed them
selves in the matter. The re-cession was not put upon the financial
needs of the valley. The appropriations have been quite sufficient
for the proper and economical administration of the trust. If made
much larger they will promote extravagant vagaries that will injure
rather than conserve the great scenery of the valley. There is no
good reason for expecting that the Federal Government would much
increase the expenditures now made by the State. So the financial
considerations may be eliminated.
The actual reason for the cession is that as long as the State
administers the valley within the Yosemite National Park there is
a divided jurisdiction, frequently working at cross purposes and
often in open antagonism. To reach the valley from any direction
visitors must pass over the National Park under Federal control.
The roads and means of access are not controlled by the State at all.
. It cannot increase the facilities of access, nor in any way affect the
regulations of the National Park that incloses its jurisdiction. Un
der these circumstances the Federal Government has been besought
to buy and improve the roads to the valley, but obviously it has no
interest in doing so. If it had jurisdiction of the valley it would
naturally have to own and maintain those roads. The State will not
spend what is necessary to buy the roads and keep them up for
they run through Federal jurisdiction. In this and a variety of other
ways the divided jurisdiction is felt as an incumbrance.
There are two ways to end it. One would be the cession of the
Yosemite National Park to the State; the other the re-cession of the
valley to the United States. We may be pardoned for saying that
there is no question of State pride at issue, though it seems to be
used to enlarge the appropriations for the valley. The valley and
the Big Trees were there before the State's trusteeship existed, and
they would stand steadfast in their place, with undiminished gran
deur, if divided jurisdiction ceased and the administration were turned
over to the United States. As it js proposed to put the national
parks in the care of the Federal Bureau of Forestry, of which Mr.
Gifford Pinchot is the head, it is probable that the administration
would be greatly improved and that California would enjoy every
advantage now offered by the valley and the trees at Federal ex
pense.
This, we take it. is a reasonable statement of the position of the
â¢ re-ceders. There is considerable hysteria in the expression of each
side in the matter. But for this there is no good reason. Of course
the parties who now enjoy the official advantages and emoluments
incident to State jurisdiction see dark plots and sinister motives in
the proposition, but the general public can afford indifference to these
visions.
In Golden Gate Park we have an example of a change of juris
diction that did not! obliterate any of the charms of that famous
spot. The Commissioners of that park, formerly appointed by the
Governor, are now appointed by the Mayor, and not a leaf has faded
nor a blade of grass grown dull in consequence. So would it be if
the United States received back the valley and the trees, and re
lieved our .State treasury of the cost of caring for them. In such
a> matter it is well to eliminate tragedy and epithet.
THE CONVERSION OF TSI AN.
j. . 1â i
IT is unofficially announced that Mr. Conger, our Minister to
China, with tin assistance of his wife, has converted the Empress
Dowager to Christianity, or at least to the cult known as Chris
tian Science, that peculiarly appeals to the Oriental mind. Tsi An
has for many years been the actual ruler of China, and up to the
conclusion of the Boxer war was stubbornly reactionary against all
reforms in that empire. After that she proceeded to relax much of
her rigor. The Manchus were ordered to intermarry with the Chi
nese, and were instructed to travel and seek education in the West
ern nations, like the Chinese have done for years. She also decreed
lhat the process of producing small feet in women should be no
longer practiced. Then she invited the wives of the foreign Ministers
to take tea with her. and unbent to a degree that was gratifying to
the legation ladies.
It was rumored that her marked preference for the society of
Mrs. Conger excited some jealousy among the ladies of the other le
gations, but the intimacy continued and is now said to have been
used by the Minister's wife in making an imperial convert to the
doctrines of Mrs. Eddy. As Christian Science is a strictly American
institution, of Boston origin, this conversion should result in the
progressive Yankeeizing of China. Whatever one may choose to
think about the Eddy religion the political effect of the adhesion of
Tsi An may be very marked. We have to advise her. however,
that it will not do to rely on Christian Science to defend her fron
tiers or preserve the territorial integrity of her empire. The land
grabbers do not care a tinker's dam, which is made of wet bread, for
mind waves, when it comes to seizing a seaport or pocketing a hin
terland. It may help her rheumatism and her often infirmities, but
guns, powder and soldiers will still be needed to keep the white
devils from gobbling up her country.
ANOTHER FROM KANSAS.
SOME irresponsible Solon of the Kansas Legislature has an
nounced his intention of submitting a bill legalizing the propa
ganda of the ten year marriage, which was advanced recently
by a respected British author now manifestly in his dotage. This
radical reformer of the social order would have it that marriages
should be regulated by the renewal of the license every ten years,
just as we give continuance to the privilege of conducting a saloon or
allowing a dog to run loose; then would the family tie be ideal ac
cording to his reckoning and the State Utopian.
We are accustomed to almost anything coming out of Kansasâ
Populism, Carrie Nation, giant pumpkins and cyclonesâthe list of
curiosities is varied and interminable. This new freak of social phil
osophy is, then, probably but a sport of the hard winter or a mani
festation of the effect of spots on the sun. In the light of former
experiences with the Kansas product of thought we can afford to be
indulgent. A joint caucus of men and women, called to con
sider the worth of this ten year marriage law, would certainly be
instructive if it did not contribute to the gayety of the nations. i

THE illustration shows the hand and wrist of Mrs. Cassie L. Chad
wick, as she bedecked them with diamond rings, bracelets and orna
ments preparatory to calling on some millionaires of whom she pro
posed borrowing an immense sum. The woman, who is a consummate ac
tress, says the Philadelphia North American, never overlooked any detail
of her "make-up." and her jeweled hand played no small part in her
game of dazzling bankers and others into the impression that she was
possessed of limitless wealth.
AURORA'S MODEL
HARRISON SMITH
IT had originally been a big. bare
garret, but under Aurora's magic it
was transformed into a decidedly
attractive studio. Tapestry hang
ings concealed the ugly outlines of un
planed studding: wide divans littered
with comfortable cushions suggested
luxurious repose; a curiously carved
cabinet stood near one of the windows.
"1 am waiting." lie said pa
tiently.
and quaint brasses, candlesticks, fend
ers and copper kettles were everywhere.
There was orderly disorder and restful
chaos.
Under the wide north light sat Auro
ra before an easel, her hair bristling
with an imposing array of scalpels and
brushes. On the canvas before her an
autumn landscape was slowly coming
into being. The landscape was not de
veloping satisfactorily, and the Bcalpels
were being pressed into service quite as
much as were the brushes.
The door opened and Paul Radford
entered. He stood apologetically by the
door smiling blandly at the girl before
the easel.
"Hello, Aurora!" said he with a
meekness becoming an intruder.
"Ccune in and shut the door," said
Aurora. "You must sit down and be
very quiet. This is my busy day."
Radford tossed his coat and hat upon
a divan, thrust his hands into his pock
ets and strode over to the easel.
Jove, Aurora, that's a stunning ma
rine," he observed.
"It's not a marine," said she testily.
"It's a landscapeâautumn."
"Oh," said he, "isn't that water in
the foreground?"
"That," said Aurora, scraping an
overdone cloud zealously, "will be a
cow when it's finished."
"I beg the cow's pardon, I'm sure."
said Radford. "I hope it's to be a red
cow, Aurora. I'm very partial to red
cows." >
"Please Bit down," she snapped impa
tiently. "You'll find some magazinesâ
somewhere about," she ended vaguely.
"Sorry, but I haven't time to look for
them," he said. "Besides, I came here
this afternoon to offer myself as a
model."
"A model for what?" she asked. "In
dolence?"
"A model of sorrow," said he.
"You don't look your part particular-
I ly." she remarked.
I "I'm consumed with it," he declared
i gloomily.
"How sad!" she said mockingly.
| Radford retired-to a divan in the cor
ner and settled comfortably on a pile
of cushions.
"If you could tra/isfer my sorrow to
i canvas your name would be made," he
1 said.
i Since when has this affliction de
scended upon you?" she asked.
"Since Tuesday afternoon at 4:30."
"Pshaw!" said Aurora, her eyes fixed
on the landscape.
it's no pshawing' matter with me,"
I said he. "Your sixth refusal has filled
j me with gloom."
"Perhaps a seventh might hearten
you a bit." she suggested maliciously,
i "There'll probably be a seventh be
fore I leave," he returned despond
ently.
Aurora laughed: then she turned
about in ncr chair and regarded him
gravely.
"Paul, why on earth can't you be
sensible?' 1 she asked. "Why can t you
good friends with me without âoh,
spoiling it all?"
"My dear Aurora." he explained sol
emnly, "if Providence had intended
men to be sensible in your presence,
be would never have given you that
cbin and those lips and "
"Bosh!" said Aurora, coloring a bit.
Kadford deserted the divan and
came over to the easel. He stood for
a time looking down at the girl
thoughtfully.
"I'll make a compact with you," he
said at length. "Tell me for the
seventh time that you won't marry me,
and I'll see to it that it is the last."
"And we'll be friends ever after?''
she said lightly.
"No, we won't be friends," he said,
"except in name."
She shrugged her shoulders, but
said nothing.
"Listen, Aurora, just say 'no' once
more and I'll take myself off. I won't
trouble you after that. I'll leave you
to your landscapesâandâandâthe
fellow who is luckier than I."
She looked up. His face was very
grave. It was unlike him to be seri
ous. She felt a trifle alarmed.
""Now, Aurora," he said quietly, "tell
me for the seventh time and I'll.go."
Aurora made a pretense of re
touching the cloud.
"I am waiting." he said patiently.
Still she said nothing. Radford
picked up his hat and coat. Then he
again took his stand by the easel.
"Are you going?" she said weakly,
j "As soon as you tell me," said he.
Aurora arose deliberately from the
J easel. She went over to the window
and began to clean her palette with a
scalpel. Radford watched her and his
eyes narrowed dangerously.
"Of course it means very little to
you," he burst out hotly, "but it seems
to me you need not delay in this use
less fashion. Y.ou might at least be
mercilful."
Aurora lifted her eyes slowly, and
as slowly her face lighted with a won
{ derful smile.
j "There are several ways of being
merciful," she said gently.
In a moment one of the brushes in
her hair had left a ghastly streak of
red on his face.

Ireland's Cofctner Mines.
Copper mining once flourished in
Ireland. The Bally Murtagh mine, in
Wicklow County, averaged 6000 tons of
copper pyrites per annum between 1840
and 1843. Desultory, prospecting is
going on at the present day in several
localities.
EXTRA VAGANCES
OF OUR SENATORS
The salary of a United States Sena
tor, like that of a member of the
House of Representatives, is $5000.
and he is allowed, in addition, mileage
at the rate of 10 cents per mile each
way between his home and the Capi
tol for each session of Congress, a pri
vate secretary at $1500 (if he is not
chairman of a. committee, in which
case he has the appointment of a
j higher salaried clerk) and $125 for
'stationery. That seems modest; but
the perquisites and emoluments not
; set down in the law are numerous and
' attractive and account in. no small
; measure for the tenacity with which
a small caliber Senator holds on when
once he has been elected.
The Senate is a small body, but It
takes a big amount of money to run
it. as the following extracts from the
last annua) appropriation bill will
show:
For compensation of Senators $+50,000
For mileaee of Senators 45,000
For expenses of the office of Vici*
President t there is none, by the
way, but Mr. Frye occupies this
room) R. 760

inquiries 20,000

Miscellaneous, not including; the ex
pense of the capitol police force,
one-half of the total ($75,000) being
paid by each house loO.ttCO
Grand total $1.227.5T::
The total of $1,227,873 tells in a gen
eral way the story of how expensive
it is to record the doings of ninety
law-makers, and to keep them com
fortable and happy for a period of
twelve months, but it is the details
that are most interesting.
In 1878 a Senator of the United
States provided himself out of the
public treasury, in addition to his sal
ary, with his mileage, his $125 for
stationery, and one clerk, if he hap
i pened to be the chairman of a com
| mittee. *That was practically all. In
! 1904 a United States Senator would
sniff at one clerk, and declare it im
possible to attend to his work without
two or three. Such a thing as paying
for a clerk out of his pocket, as his
predecessors, up to a comparatively
few years ago. were accustomed to do,
would be regarded by the Senator of
to-day as a crime.âDecember Pear
son's.
HOW B*l EVEN SOX
VIEWED WOMAX
Mrs. Kate Leslie Smith has contrib
uted a most attractive article to yie
! January number of the Bnoklovers
I Magazine on "Stevenson's View of
\ Woman." Mrs. Smith says that "to
, form an opinion of Stevenson's attitude
to women in generalâor woman in
| particularâfrom a critical study of his
i heroines, would be making the greatest
possible mistake." His heroines are
disappointing. "That he loved his
mother devotedly, we know well from
his letters," continues Mrs. Smith, "and
yet when he named his heroines, what
a phenomenal procession of motherless
girls! Kirstie Elliott, Catriona. Bar
j bara Grant. Flora Gilchrist, Blanche de
Maletrolt. and many another. How
frequently he testifies to the success
and happiness of his own marriage; yet
;we look in vain in his books for pic
tures of connubial bliss, and must con
tent ourselves with sketches of Jim
Pinkerton and his bride In 'The Wreck
er.' Monsieur and Madame Berthelini in
'Providence and the Guitar.' and the
generous struggle of Keawe and his
Kokua to buy the bottle from each
other in 'The Bottle Imp.' "
Stevenson owed much to women: to
his nurse, to his wife, and to his f lend
Mrs. Sitwell, who first made him be
lieve in his own powers. He made
grateful acknowledgment of his in
debtedness. His attitude to women
might be summed up in two words:
: detached chivalry. His sympathies
were artistic as well as personal, and
to quote Charles Warren Stoddard.
! "his intimacies were born and bred
! above the ears."
Words of Praise for Us.
More than any other country of the
; present time, with the possible excep
i tion of the Russian empire, says the
Saturday Review, the United States
â¢ may be regarded as a complete homo
! geneous economic entity. It is able to
i grow all the corn it requires, it can*
: raise all the live stock that it needs,
i its cotton plantations are sufficient to
supply all its requirements, its mineral
resources both on base and precious
metals are extensive, and its coal mines
i are inexhaustible.
Add to this every year enormous ac
cessions by immigration of carefully
selected adult able-bodied and skillful
workmen to assist in the development
of these very varied resources. The
development of that country is prob
ably due in large degree to these
causes. We may be sure that, in the
future, it will become more and more
independent of all other countries.
World's Best Timepiece.
! The world's best timepice, an elec
tric clock in the basement of the Ber
lin Observatory, was installed by
Professor Foerster in 1865, and has
often run two or three months with
an average daily deviation of only
15-1000 of a second. To improve even
this marvelous performance, the as
tronomers in charge are arranging to
place the air-tight glass case in an un
derground vault, securing freedom
from changes of temperature and air
pressure.
Oldest Sexton on Earth.
Charles E. Pope of Sandwich. Mass..
is the oldest sexton in the country, if
not In the world. He has rung the
bells of the First Parish church of
that town for nearly three-quarters of
a century. Mr. Pope was born July 11,
1820, and was made sexton at a very
early age. Mr. Pope is a most remark
able old gentleman, and, unlike the
average sexton, he is an educated man.

Mrs. Lena Johnson, who stole some
articles of trifling value from one of the
big department stores in which she was
ostensibly shopping wept so piteously
in court that Judge Fritz fined her only
$20. Her husband, Otto, also shed tears,
and there was moisture in the voice of
the attorney that pleaded for leniency.
It was her first arrest for stealing and
she earnestly promised to steal no more.

ities. Patrolman Slattery told Judge

Blx young fellows were arraigned
before Judge Mogan on the charge
of burglary, filed by Patrolmen O'Con
nor and Blick, neither of whom ap
peared to prosecute. The defendants,
however, were permitted to tell how
it came to pass, and their story made
even Bailiff Mahoney laugh.
They boarded the roof of a freight
car at San Jose with intent to steal
a ride to this city, but had not been
there long ere the chilling wind
prompted them to seek a warmer re-
Ireat. So at the train's first stopping
place they descended from their perch
and entered a car, carefully faatened
the door and prepared to spend the
remainder of their journey in com
parative comfort. No sooner had the
train started, however, than they dis
"v>red they were inmates of a re
frigerator car, and It was only by
â¢lancing to prevent their blood freez
ing that they were enabled to reach
San Francisco alive, as the train did
not again atop until it rolled into the
yard at Third and Townsend streets.
.So great was their hurry to get out
that they threw caution to the winds,
and that's how they came to be ar
rested. *

cafe and challenged Mr. Jackson's bar
tender, one Len Harris (likewise col
ored), to fight to the death, but Mr.
Harris ran upstairs and remained there
until Mr. Waddell departed in quest
of Mr. Jackson, whom he preferred to
carve. Soon afterward Mr. Harris, who
had buckled on a fully-loaded revolver,
was seated at dinner, consisting of
j roast chicken and fixings, behind the
bar when Mr. Waddell re-entered and
renewed his Invitation to fight. Mr.
Harris made threatening display of his
shooting iron, but Mr. Waddell laughed
it to scorn. â¢
"Ah'll jes take dat yah pill box ob
yourn." quoth Mr. Waddell, "en gib to
mah sistah's baby fo' to play with. So
nan' it obah heah."
Mr. Harris weakened and inconti
nently fled, leaving his dinner, which
Mr. Waddell devoured as spoils of war.
He was enjoying the final mouthful
and preparing to pick nts teeth with
the wishbone when Tillman Baxter
! J. . . .T.
(tan-colored), a friend of the absent
Mr. Jackson, entered and inquired as
to that gentleman's whereabouts.
"Ah doan know whafo' he am." said
Mr. Waddell, unfolding his razor, "but
you'll do jes as well In his place."
With a whoop Mr. Waddell leaped at
Mr. Baxter, who rushed to the street
shouting for help.
"Doan waste yo' win' a-yellin, njg
gah," shouted Mr. Waddell, in close
pursuit, "fo' you'll sho'ly need all de
bref in yo' body 'foah dis yah chase am
finished. Ah'll git yo' shuah."
Mr. Waddell was hunting his quarry
around a street car when Patrolman
Bruce grabbed and held him, very
much to the relief of Mr. Baxter.
"Ah'll have mah 'fense ready ter
morrah," said Mr. Waddell to Judge
Conlan. i
"And a strong one you'll need to keep
you out of Jail," answered Judge Con
lan.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL. FRIDAY, JANUARY 6, 1905.
MRS. METCALFE
GETS REVOLVER
Presents Order for Fire Arm
With Which Keeley After
ward Shoots Her Husband
SAMPLES AID CULPRIT
They Neglect to Inform the
Police While He Changes
Clothes in Their Rooms
At the inquest yesterday on
the death of Edward Bruce Metcalfe
the fact was brought out in the testi
mony that Mrs. Metcalfe presented to
Mrs. William Sample of 603 Eddy street
an order for the revolver with which
Edward Pierce Keeley afterward, on
December 29, murdered her husband. It
was also shown that Mr. and Mrs. Sam
ple shielded Keeley from arrest and as
sisted him to escape after he had con
fessed to them that he had murdered
Metcalfe.
Mr. and Mrs. Sample testified that
shortly before 2 o'clock, less than an
hour after the shooting, Keeley had
come to their rooms and said, "I have
done it."
"What have you done?" asked Mrs.
Sample.
"I shot Bruce," replied Keeley.
Keeley had then explained that he
went to Metcalfe's room at 18 Seventh
street and heard somebody crying. He
then knocked on the door and it was
opened. The room was in darkness, and
Keeley drew his revolver and shot Met
calfe. He heard Metcalfe fall and then
left the building.
Mr. and Mrs. Sample, leaving Keeley
in their rooms, went to 18 Seventh
street. They passed two or three
patrolmen on the way, but did not tell
them that Keeley was the murderer
and that he was then in their rooms.
"Why did you not inform those po
licemen that the murderer was in your
rooms?*' inquired a Juror.
"Becauseâwell, I didn't inform
them," replied Mrs. Sample, giving "the
woman's reason."
When they returned to their house
Keeley was still in their rooms. He had
shaved off his mustache and put on a
pair of blue overalls over his trousers.
Then, Mrs. Sample said, she ordered
him out of the house, and he went.
Sample told about a revolver given
him by a cousin by marriage whose
present whereabouts was unknown to
him. He had offered the revolver to
Keeley for $1, as he did not want to
have it lying about the house, and had
given Keeley an order on Mrs. Sample
for the weapon. When Keeley present
ed the order Mrs. Sample could not find
the key of her trunk, and he went away
without it. Several days later Mrs.
Metcalfe presented the order, and Mrs.
Sample gave Keeley the revolver the
next time he called. That was a few
days before the murder.
Mr. and Mrs. Sample testified that
they had always known Mrs. Metcalfe
as Mrs. Keeley and supposed that she
was Keeley's wife.
The jury returned a verdict charging
Edward Pierce Keeley with the murder
of Edward Bruce Metcalf* 3 on Decem
ber 29. â¢

SHOTS FRIGHTEN
HOTEL GUESTS
Bellboy at the Berkshire
Fires at Another and Two
of the BuUets Take Effect
WOUNDS NOT DANGEROUS
Assailant Surrenders Him
self and Declares the
Shooting Was Accidental
A shooting affray at the Berkshire
Hotel, 711 Jones street, yesterday
morning caused the wildest excitement
for a few minutes among the guests.
The participants were William Grant,
head bellboy, and Robert Drake, one of
-his assistants, both colored. Grant
was wounded in the hand and leg and
was taken to the Central Emergency
Hospital. Drake surrendered himself
to the police.
Grant and Drake quarreled on
Wednesday night and while Drake was
going upstairs carrying a tray Grant
struck him. Drake put the tray down
to defend himself, but Grant took
refuge in the elevator.
The manager of thÂ» hotel on hear
ing of the occurrence discharged Grant
yesterday morning. Grant went to his
room to pack up and Drake was ad
vised to keep away from him till he
had left the hotel. Miss E. Kincfdd, a
waitress, had given Grant two skirts
to get cleaned and she went to his
room to ask him where he had taken
them.
While she and Grant were talking
Drake made his appearance and said
to Grant, "I want to see you." Grant
replied, "I have seen all I want of
you," and closed his door. Drake, who
had a stick in his left hand and had
his right hand in his hip pocket, pushed
the door open. Just then Miss Klncaid
turned and walked away. She heard
three shots fired In quick succession,
but did rÂ»ot know who fired them.
The police were at once notified and
Detective Ed. Gibson was detailed on
the case. He sent Grant to the Cen
tral Emergency Hospital and while the
detective was making inquiries at the
hotel Drake surrendered himself at
police headquarters. He declared that
Grant had drawn the revolver and that
during a struggle for its possession it
was discharged three times. One bul
let struck Grant on the hand, another
in the leg and the third struck the
wall. The police discredit Drake's
story. He was locked up pending de
velopments.
WYMAN TRIAL STILL DRAGS
IN JUDGE LAWLOR'S COURT
Justice's Comment on Point Advanced
by Attorney Countryman Rouses
Some Little Interest.
The progress of the Wyman trial
was as slow yesterday as it was the
day preceding. The only moment
when any interest was displayed was
when Judge Lawlor said, in reply to
a point advanced by Attorney Coun
tryman, "The case you have cited does
not point that crime cannot be
committed, even If the proceedings
connected with the election be found
faulty."
The morning session and most of
the afternoon were consumed in prov
ing the validity of the petitions of the
parties in the primary election in
question. Countryman fought every
inch, objecting on every possible tech
nicality. One of his objections w T as
sustained by the court.
He contended that the notice of
election was not published three times
in a daily paper, fifteen days before
the time of election. This was sus
tained, and the notice of election was
not entered as evidence. Several times
during the day Attorneys Countryman
and Hosmer treaded on the borders
of repartee. They were admonished
by Judge Lawlor. who hinted that
there was too much talk wasted in
the case.
The roster of voters in the Seventy
third Precinct, where the felony is al
leged to have been committed, was
produced just at the close of the af
ternoon session. The prosecution will
probably offer evidence intended to
show the actual commission of the
crime to-day.
Says Drummer Was Idle.
Tlllmann & Bendel yesterday filed
an answer and cross-complaint to the
suit of F. P. Katz, a whisky drummer,
who demanded 59125 25 damages be
cause he had been discharged before
the expiration of his contract. He was
to be paid $6500 for the first year and
$7500 for the second, and entered
upon his duties in August, 1903. In
May, 1904, he was discharged, and
thereupon he sued. The firm, in its
answer, says he was not discharged
without reason, but from September,
1903. to the following May had idled
away his time, lost many customers
for the firm and refused to abide by
the company's regulations. On this
account, in its counter claim, the com
pany sues him for $15,000.

SLOAN TO WEAR CORRIGAN'S
GREEN AND WHITE COLORS
Famous Jockey Expects to*Be Rein
stated in This Country on the
French Court's Decision.
CHICAGO. Jan. s.âTod Sloan, the
much talked of jockey, and, as his
friends declare, the much abused
youth of the saddle, is in this city. He
is, according to Ed Corrigan, to ride
for the latter next season, providing
the turf body in control gives him a
license. That he will get one is based
on the opinion of the French court's
decision that Sloan is entitled tp $40,
--000 for the injury done in ruling him
off by the French Jockey Club. The
feeling here is that Sloan is guiltless
of any turf crime abroad.
Sloan's appearance in the saddle
again will be watched with much in
terest to see if he has lost any of
his old time skill, judgment of pace
and ability to get a horse to run kind
ly for him.
CHOKER'S HORSES CANNOT
BE TRAINED AT NEWMARKET
Jockey Club Officials Refuse Per
mission, but as Usual Give
No Reason for the Action.
NEWMARKET, England, Jan. 5.â
The Jockey Club has refused to grant
permission for the horses of Richard
Croker recently purchased In Ireland
to train at Newmarket. As usual the
club officials gave no reason for its
action.
The secretary wrote to J. E. Brewer
of Heath House at Newmarket, with
whom Mr. Crocker had placed three
of his Irish horses for training, that
he would not be permitted to train
there. The three horses are still at
Newmarket.

APPLIANCE CO., 0 OFarrell street
Mansard Meets With
Poor Racing Luck
v and Gets Place.
Smithy Kane Awakes
With a Start and
Nearly Wins.
Jakie Jones didn't accomplish much
with Misty's Pride in the five-furlong j
purse event yesterday at Oakland.
Jakie doesn't appear able to do much
with any of the short-priced ones he
rides of late. Pretty soon his services
will not be in such big demand.
The Oregon mare was played down
from 8 to i to 13 to 10 and ran fourth.
She did not run her race. Sad Sam, a
7 to 1 chance, after cutting off and foul
ing almost the whole field on the far
turn slid in less than a length ahead of
Mansard in 1:00%. As the race was run
the latter sprinter should have won, for
when cut off by Sad Sam Davis had to
pull him up, losing at least three
lengths. The favorite lost show honors
to Martinmas.
FOUR OUTSIDERS WIN.
Outsiders captured the first four num
bers on the card, then two choices
reached home in front. The track was
in excellent condition and a large
crowd viewed the sport.
Mimo, the mount of Travers, had first
call in the betting in the opening six
furlong event, and the gelding finished
second to Mocorito. The latter mare re
ceived careful nursing at the hands of
McÃride, and coming from behind out
gamed the first choice, winning by
nearly a length. Hipponax finished a
close third, in front of The Cure.
There were fourleen starters for the
seven-furlong selling run. which fol
lowed. Suburban Queen looked the most
eligible, but Knapp could not drop the
mare in among the first three. The win
ner turned up in Karabel. a 13 to 1 shot,
which scored, ridden out, from Cinna
bar and Haven Run. Suburban Queen
died away after looking to have a great
chance.
ROBERT MITCHELL HAS SPEED.
Robert Mitchell, with Travers in the
saddle, made an awful showing of his
company in the six-furlong sprint for
three-year-olds. The Kismet colt re
ceded in the betting to 8 to 1 and then
went out and won in stake horse
fashion, scampering in nine lengths be
fore Sweet Kitty Bellairs, a 50 to 1
shot. Princess Zula, the 2 to 1 favor
ite, with Helgesen up, ran a fair
third.
A dozen cheap platers lined up in
the mile and seventy yard selling num
ber, .riugie, with Davip up, closed an
11 to 5 choice and with little in re
serve downed Adirondack less than a
length. McÃride finished third on
Rene, a3O to 1 outsider. Frank Woods,
the mount of Bullman, the opening
favorite, came in for steady support,
but the old horse appears to be all in.
After running two bloomers Smithy
Kane came out for a "fair dinkum"
in the concluding five and a half fur
long dash. The fireside folks had the
temerity to back the Texas halfbreed
to beat Prince Brutus, a 1 to 2 fav
orite, and the good thing nearly went
through. Smithy Kane unwound a ball
of speed which was much longer than
any one was led to suspect he pos
sessed. Jakie Jones, who rode the fav
orite, tumbled to this fact when the
stretch was reached and through dint
of hard work got the Prince up in
time to win by a neck. David Boland
was third.

M. J. Daly purchased Arcade from
Walter Jennings yesterday for $1000.
Puerl Wilkerson, now in Los Angeles,
claims to hold a contract oh the ser
vices of "Big BIN" Knapp, the jockey,
tVhich does not expire until March 26
next. The jockey's father affirms that
no such contract exists. Wilkerson
filed his claim with Judge Hamilton
of Ascot Park. Knapp Sr. and his son
left for Los Angeles last evening to
settle up the matter. Walter Jen
nings recently engaged Knapp to do
the riding for his stable, and he states
that even if Wilkerson does hold any
sort of a contract on Knapp it is null
and void, tor Wilkerson owes the
jockey 5200 in unpaid riding fees.

following dates at a meeting held yes
terday: Seattle, May 27 to July 19,
inclusive; Portland, July 22 to Sep
tember 16, inclusive. Kenilworth Park.

WITHDRAWS ORDINANCE
RELATING TO CREMATION
Health Board Finds Proposed Measure
Conflicts With State LawâPer
mits Granted for Sanitariums.
The Supervisors' Health Committee
yesterday listened to Health Officer
Ragan and other officials of the
Health Department in support of the
proposed ordinance making it unlaw
ful to cremate the remains of any dead
person until they have been in a re
ceiving vault for at least thirty days.
Dr. Ragan said that the State law re
quires remains to be kept five days
before cremation and as the proposed
ordinance conflicts f with the statute
he asked to withdraw the measure.
This was allowed.
Dr. Ragan was heard regarding the
objections of the Health Board to the
establishment of a laundry at York
and Twenty-sixth streets and the rec
ommendation that soap factories and
tanneries be excluded from the district
near the swamp at Twenty-sixth and
Army streets. Ragan stated that such
industries were a menace to health,
owing to there being no sewer connec
tion. The committee laid the matter
over pending the making of some ar
rangement to connect the York-street
sewer with the Army-street sewer. The
Board of Works was asked to make
an estimate of the cost of the im
provement.
The committee was kept busy hear
ing petitions for permits to conduct
sanitariums and maternity hospitals.
Permits were granted to various in
stitutions that complied with the or
dinance, while a few that the com
mittee was doubtful about were taken
under advisement.
DEMENTED MAN OFFERS
FORTUNE FOR AN APPLE
Martin Britt Would Pay a Thousand
Dollars for Fruit, But Gen
erosity is Spurned.
Martin Britt, a demented but good
natured man walked up to Deputy
Health Officer Louis Levy yesterday
afternoon and demanded that Levy
sell him an apple for a thousand dol
lars. Levy was unable to accommo-
Sate Mr. Britt, but escorted him to
the insane ward of the Central Emerg
ehcy Hospital where he told his trou
bles. . 1
Among other things, Britt told
about a $50,000,000 deposit to his
credit in the Hibernia Bank, donated
to him by H. E. Huntington. He pre
sented a check for the amount and
when it was dishonored, he felt sore.
Britt will probably be sent to one of
the State hospitals for the insane to
day. He says his home is in Oakland.

Haird in the Cross ley building by Herbert
on a claim of $82
COUNSEL CANNOT SHAKE
GALLAND'S TESTIMONY
Attorneys for George Monnier Elicit
Nothing New From Deputy
Sheriff.
The damaging testimony given by
Jesse Galland, one-time bosom friend
of George Monnier, who is suing for
a share of his father's estate, was
carefully gone over by Sullivan and
Roche, for the contestant, yesterday.
Galland's testimony as to the abuse of
the old man by George and the re
quest that he endeavor to get George
"shanghaied" withstood all technical
assaults by the lawyers.
During the testimony some personal
questions relative to the witness' con
nection with politics were asked, and
Galland said he had made money at it
in the past and stood pretty well at
present.
Miss Marie Labarcat and Miss Marie
Vidaillet, servants in the Monnier
household, told of the Quarrels and
bitter language occurring frequently,
and the latter witness said she had
seen George choke his father and
chase him with a butcher knife.
Mrs. Monnier was on the stand for
half an hour under a fire of questions
from Sullivan. She refused to tell her
age, and Sullivan insisted. George D.
Collins strenuously objected to her
answering, but Judge Graham allowed
the question, and Mrs. Monnier said
that she was born in 1847 and came to
California when she was 16. She tes
tified that she failed to remember
where she lived in San Francisco be
fore her marriage. The case will be
continued Monday morning at 11
o'clock.
COME FAR TO TESTIFY
IN A BURGLARY CASE
Four Army Officers Arrive from Fort
Snelling as Witnesses Against
Juan Sanchez.
Captains J. A. Pearce, E. G. Over
shine and I. J. Carr and Lieutenant S.
A. Price of the Twenty-eighth Infan
try arrived yesterday from Fort Snell
ing, Minnesota, to give testimony
against Juan Sanchez, a former Fili
pino scout, who is to be tried in the
United States District Court on a
charge of burglarizing the house of
Captain Pearce at the Presidio in
September. Sanchez was captured by
Captain Carr. On the person of the
prisoner was found the loot, consisting
of jewelry and valuables. He had en
tered several houses at the post.
Inasmuch as the crime was commit
ted on the Goverment reservation the.
prosecution devolved on the Federal
authorities. The cost of bringing the
four officers to San Francisco will
amount to about $1000, whereas the
total amount of Sanchez's, thefts could
not have been more than $250 j

WIFE OF A RAILROAD
MAN IS GIVEN DIVORCE
Mrs. Nettie Taylor's Tale of Cruelty
Induces Judge Graham to**-
Grant Decree.
Judge Graham yesterday granted a
divorce to Nettie J. Taylor from Mar
vin L. Taylor, a Southern Pacific con
ductor, on the ground of cruelty. Their
property interests were settled out of
court. Mrs. Taylor's complaint was
sensational in its charge that her hus
band had boasted that he had killed
two persons and had "kicked the life
out of his passengers," and was ready
to kick the life out of her. She also
said he pinched the nose of his step
daughter until it produced a growth
on that member. The mother and
daughter testified to these and other
facts in court.
Other divorces were granted as fol
lows: By Judge Graham, to Lillian
Mitchell from Francis Mitchell, deser
tion; by Judge Troutt, to Marthie
Downing from Warren Downing, de
sertion; by Judge Kerrigan to Agnes
Kellow from Thomas Kellow, neglect.
A. E. Stanley sued her husband,
James F. Stanley, for divorce on the
ground of cruelty. In her long list
of allegations was one to the effect
that four years ago he refused to pro
vide a wardrobe for an expected
babe, with the remark that "it would
not be the first baby born without
clothes."
Other actions for separation were
begun by Peer Ernest Segerstrom
against Helma. Segerstrom for deser
tion; JosieÂ» Lenterdt against Christo
pher Lenterdt, neglect; Frank A. Roux
against Sarah C. Roux, desertion; Ed
ward J. Thompson against Mary
Thompson, habitual intemperance;
John Donnachie against Maria Don
nachie, infidelity.
Accused of Perjury.
Mrs. H. M. Wright, 32A Rausch
street, obtained a warrant from Po
lice Judge Conlan yesterday for the
arrest of Warren G. Grove, a petty
officer at Mare Island, on a charge of
perjury. It is alleged that Grove, dur
ing his examination in a divorce suit
brought by his wife and tried in
Judge Troutt's court on December 2.
swore that he had no money or means
except $7 due him next pay day.
\Â»hereas he $565 in the bank of
tile United States Steamship Inde
pendence at Mare Island.

ANOTHER HITCH
IN PROGRAMME
Gardner Refuses to Come
Here for the Pleasure
of Mixing It With Gans
DOUBLE EVENT PLANNED
Neil and Murphy and Finn
ucane and Hyland Are On
the Bill as Substitutes
The fight layout for this month was
switched again last night. Jimmy
Gardner does not feel disposed to come
all the way to San Francisco just for
the pleasure of a mere mix-up with
Gans. He informed Manager Greggains
of his decision by wire last night, and
the latter set about matching Frankie
| Neil, the bantam, with Tommy Murphy
of Philadelphia, and Dick Hyland with
| Billy Finnucane of Chicago as a double
! event in place of the Gans-Gardner go.;
The outlook for a "fight" has become
! quite a joke along the line. The news
! that the Gans-Gardner bout was off did
. not create a great sensation at that.
When the match was made last Mon
day night there were many who took it
as a joke, and still others declared it
was another deeply laid plot by which
Herford might get away with some
more California coin. Many of the wise
[ fraternity are sure the Gans-Gardner
match was made in Baltimore by the
wily Herford.
Gardner says in his telegram that he
has a fight on at Denver on the 25th
inst., and therefore could not get to
this city in time to go a few rounds
with Gans. No one seems to know the
true reason why the match was de
clared off, though Herford might have
had something to say about that, too.
Gans and Herford will come .right
through to San Francisco just as
though nothing had happened. They
do not seem to be the least bit afraid
of meeting a lynching party or a vigil
ance committee at the ferry. Their
mission here will probably be explained
when something- comes off and a big
wad of the public's coin finds its way
into their capacious pockets.
Murphy and Neil were practically
matched by Greggains last night. Both
boys say they are eager for a go, and
there seems no hitch in the proceed
ings. Murphy's manager is willing to
post a forfeit of $2500 and let this sum
go for a side bet. Neil says he is ready
to bet Murphy any amount he desires.
The Hyland-Finnucane bout was
not settled last night. Hyland is will
ing to go on, but no word was received
from the Chicago lad. He is rated as a
fast boy, having met such men as Tom
my Mowatt, Willie Mack. Tommy Sul
livan and others. Greggains thinks the
double match system will make a hit.
Both mills will be twenty-round affairs.
Morris Levy is trying to sign Nelson
and Young Corbett for a return bout
before the Hayes Valley Club in Febru
ary. It is almost an assured fact that
the match will go through. Corbett
wants to fight and is at present in
training. Nelson will be about ready to
talk business when he returns from his
hunting trip some few weeks hence.
The men will likely be signed at 130
pounds at 6 o'clock, the weight they
met at before. There is practically no
other match in sight for the promoters.

Delegates to the Master Painters' and

Many Interesting Points.
The delegates to the Master Painters'
and % Decorators' convention were
guests of a well-known wholesale
paint house on an excursion yesterday
around the bay. The party left the
Clay-street wharf early in the morn
ing in one of the bay steamers. All
the interesting points about the bay
were visited. The day was chilly, but
it did not have the effect of marring
the pleasure of the members. Plenty
to eat and drink added to the enjoy
ment of the party.
At the opening session of the con
vention Wednesday, at Red Men's
Hall, many interesting papers, dealing
with vital points of the craft, were
read and discussed.
At the session to-day many impor
tant resolutions will be brought before
the body to vote on., and it is expected
that some of them relating to existing
conditions will be adopted.

pointed:

JEWISH WOMEN DEBATE
OBSERVANCE OF CHRISTMAS
Council Meeting at Temple Emanu-El
Listens to Able Papers on
Interesting Topic.
The Council of Jewish Women held
a, very interesting convention yester
day afternoon in the rooms of Tem
ple Emanu-El. The subject, "Should
We Celebrate Christmas in/ Our
Homes?" was ably and pointedly dis
cussed. Two papers, by Mrs. J. C.
Levey and Mrs. D. Hirshberg, were
favorably inclined toward the observ
ance of Christmas, stating that it in
no wise harmed nor interfered with
the Jewish faith. The negative was
emphatically upheld by Mrs. M. C.
Sloss and Mrs. Â«.M. Neumann, the
latter particularly protesting against
the introduction of Christian rites
into the households of Judaism.
Much enthusiasm greeted each
reader and the papers were excellent
in argument and reason.

FIGHTING CLUBS
TO BE PROBED
Supervisors Will Investi
gate the Iniquitous In
stitutions Next Week
BERGER IS UNDER FIRE
Olympic flub Directors to
Dispose of His Case To-
Night at Their Meeting
The Supervisors' Police Committee
postponed action yesterday on the peti
tion of the Hawthorne Club to give an
amateur boxing exhibition on the 31st
inst. The committee decided to await
the outcome of the agitation raised by
the Olympic Club relative to the al
leged irregularities of boxers in violat
ing the amateur rules. The committee
cited representatives of all the ama
teur clubs to appear next Thursday,
when an inquiry into the alleged viola
tions of the rules will be commenced.
The Brooklyn Athletic Club, through
E. C. Harrington, manager, filed aft ap
plication to give a professional boxing
exhibition during March.
President William Greer Harrison of
the Olympic Club andr George James
and other officials of the Pacific Ath
letic Association will be among the
men who will endeavor to show the Su
pervisors the deplorable state into
which the so-called amateur boxing
has fallen in this city. While many
people know of the existing conditions
it is difficult to get them to testify,
especially as the promoters stand to
gether and deny that money is paid,
although it is a matter of common no
toriety.
The directors of the Olympic Club
will take up the Berger case to-night
at their meeting. Although powerful
influences are at work to save him, it is
expected the. resignation he placed in
the hands of the directors some months
since will be accepted. He claims that
at the worst he is in the same category
as Jimmie Britt, Eddie Graney, Phil
Wand, Young Mitchell and others who
are professionals and who are allowed
to remain in the club. He overlooks
the fact that charges of conduct preju
dicial to the best interests of the club
have been preferred against him, while
there is nothing against the others.

'he early morning tides are given In the left t
hand column and the successive tides of the j
.<iay in the order of occurrence as to time; the
'â  unh time column gives the latt tide of tha

Files Charge Against Policeman.
Attorney John T. Carroll filed a
charge yesterday afternoon with the
Board of Police Commissioners against
Policeman Frederick A. Daurenheim.
accusing him of being intoxicated and
insulting him. Carroll says he went
into a saloon about 1 o'clock yester
day afternoon on the corner of Dun
bar alley and Merchant street look
ing for another attorney. Dauren
heim, who was there in citizen's dress,
made an offensive remark and when
Carroll told him to attend to his own
business he showed his star and grab
bed hold of Carroll, beginning to.
search his pockets, and declaring Car- !
roll a suspicious character. Carroll
was taken before Sergeant Moffitt at
the Central Station and on giving his
name and address was allowed to go.

CLUNIE ACCUSES
TWO EXECUTORS.
Objections to Account Are
Full of Charges of Mal
administration of Estate
INVESTIGATION BEGINS
.
Allegations Read in Court
and B. G. \Vhite Submits
to Kigorous Examination
Sensational r bjections to tbe aecourt
| ing of E. A. liridgford and Burrell G.
! White for their administration of the
million-dollar estate of the late Thomas
!J. Clunie were read in Judge Coffey's
court yesterday by Andrew J. Clunie.
' the third executor and brother 'of
the deceased. The written objections
bristled with the words "looting,"
; "misappropriation," "grossly false" and
| "illegal." and in the course of a search
ing examination of White, which was
unfinished at the end of the afternoon,
Mr. Clunie added "conspiracy" to his
; list of charges against the two txec
j utors.
! Bridgford and White were both in
: court and were represented in the pro-
I ceedings by Hudson Grant, former
! Juda;e at Woodland, whose employment
as attorney by the executors is one of
the chief grounds of Clunie's attack
1 upon the account. D. M. Delmas was
! also present, but his interest is in a
matter that will be taken up laterâ
that or his fee as attorney for the es
tate. In the examination of White
some of the questions were not gone
j into because of the absence of certain
receipts arid documents that he will be
required to produce in court on next
: Monday afternoon.
t'lunie especially excepts to certain
; items of receipts and moneys on deposit
that Mill be investigated when the rec
, ords are produced. These include
the distribution to Florence Clunie ol
$40.325 received from Andrew Clunie on
I the settlement of her contest. Andrew
jJ. Clunie says this was never paid to
j the executors as such, but was the
property of Florence Clunie, held by
I the trustees.
DISCREPANCY EXPLAINED.
An exception was made to the report
Of the sale of personal property for
$12,862 SO,-when the arriount was really
$19,480. White admitted that there was
a mistake in the entry, but said it was
explained by another entry for the sale
of sugar stock, whi< h covered the dis
crepancy. Objection was made to a
charge for the keep of a horse which,
Clunie said, was used by White "for
his private amusement and con
venience." White hotly denied this,
and at this point declared that the ob
jections were "blasphemous and out
rageous," and that he did not want a
day to pass without a full investiga
tion.
Exception was also taken to the pay
ment ot $150,000 to Mrs. Clunie under a
decree #f partial distribution on Feb
ruary 5 last, on the ground that "no
such decree was ever made and no such
payments could have been legally
made."
In examining White on the settle
ment of Mrs. Clunie's contest. Andrew
J. Clunie asked him if he had selected
John R. Wilson as Mrs. Clunie's attor
ney. He replied that he had only men
tioned him in connection with getting
an allowance for Mrs. Clunie. Clunie
brought out that White and Wilson
were warm friends, and then asked
why White had not employed him as
his own attorney. White declined to
answer, and Judge Coffey sustained his
objection, whereupon Clunie remarked
that he wanted to show that White
brought about all the trouble with Mrs.
Ciunie and sent attorneys to her.
Pursuing the same line he attempted
to go into the relationship between
White and Bridgford for the purpose,
he said, of showing a "conspiracy to
vote away the money of this estate."
White was very close to General Clunie
and attended to all his business. He
was present when the will was made
and read the items to the testator. This
led to a line of questioning upon
White's construction of a clause in the
will appointing three attorneys as
executors and instructing that the
three should choose one/of their num
ber to act as attorney ia the adminis
tration.
HIRED ATTORNEYS.
White said that he and Bridgford at
first thought that much legal service
would not be necessary in the adminis
tration, but upon Clunie's assertion
that there would be a contest of the
will they concluded to get assistance.
Bridgford wanted Hudson Grant to rep
resent him and White wanted W. H.
Robinson. Both were employed, Grant
preparing nearly all the papers in the
estate and Robinson passing on them
for White. A few weeks ago it was
agreed that Grant should have $8000
for his services and Robinson $2000.
These amounts were paid and Clunie's
objection thereto says: "It was wholly
unwarranted by law; paid without
services having been rendered in return
and paid and accepted with the full
knowledge that the payment constitut
ed a looting of the estate and a mis
appropriation of the money." Clunie
declared that the services were not
worth $500 and that the payments were
net for the attorneys, but were to be
shared by the executors. Questioning
White on this point and in regard to
the checks which were cashed at the
Western National Bank, of which
Bridgford is a director, Clunie said he
would demand an inspection of Grant's
private account at that bank.
It was further charged that the law
yers were paid out of the receipts from
the sale of personal property, permis
sion to sell which had been obtained
by representing that there were no
funds from which to pay legacies.
White admitted that the money was
so paid, and that he had told some one
that he had no funds to pay legacies.
Clunie also objected to an item of
$2500 "in the hands of E. A. Bridg
ford," and charged that it had been
paid as an advance on commissions,
contrary to the rule of the court. White
would not swear that this was or was
not true, but said there was an un
derstanding that the money would be
repaid. Clunie declared that there
should be no commissions, because
there was no labor in administering the
estate and "upon the grounds that the
executors have willfully misappropriat
ed the property of the estate."
Clunie said that D. M. Delmas was
employed as attorney for the estate,
according to the report, but he be
lieved he was employed by Bridgford
because the latter waa fearful of losing
his position as trustee.
Says Blasting Causes Earthquakes.
John F. English sent a letter to the
Supervisors yesterday stating that he
has been informed that the recent
earthquake shocks are really caused
by the blacting operations on the
Southern Pacific bay shore line.
English suggests that the blasting be
done in the daytime, so people can
sleep at night. -

LEE ASSERTS
HIS INNOCENCE
Denies He Passed a Raised
Bill Upon Mrs. Vallade to |
Pay for the Macaroni
SHE SWEARS 'TWAS HE
Prosecution Oifers to Prove
Defendant Was Once Ar-;
i
rested on Forgery Charge
Much contradictory testimony was
given yesterday before United States
Commissioner Heacock in the prelim
inary examination of Le Roy Lee,
charged with passing a $1 silver cer
tificate raised to $10 on Mrs. A. J. Val
lade. proprietor of a delicatessen store
at 114>_ Sixth street, on December 26.
Mrs. Vallade and her daughter Grace
positively Identified Lee as the man
who had passed the raised note. They
said he had been In the store several
times before that and had bought sev
eral articles.
Miss Grace Vallade told the court
that at 5 o'clock on the afternoon of
the 26th Lee and his wife came in and
bought 5 cents' worth of potato salad,
for which he paid with a 50-cent piece.
Lloyd Lee. their 12-year-old son, was
no-t with them. In fact Miss Grace and
her mother were positive that they had
never seen the boy until he appeared ir.
the courtroom. At half-past 6 o'clock
Lee had returned alone and bought of
Miss Grace 10 cents' worth of macaroni
cakes, giving her the bad 810 bill.
On the evening c-f the 28th she saw
him pass the store, and she and the
dishwasher followed htm until they saw
him go into 934 Howard street, where
he roomed. They then Informed Po
liceman T. B. Riordan, who. arrested
Lee.
Lee and his wife swore that they.
\Â« Hh the boy, bought two mince pies
and pome doughnuts on the occasion
reterred to and that Lee paid with a
$5 bill, which bill was genuine. This
bill Mrs. Vallade admitted having re
ceived but at an earlier date than the
one testified to. The boy. Lloyd Lee,
12 years old. swore that he saw his
lather give a $5 bill. In answer to a
question by Judge Heacock the boy
said he had not spoken with his
parents at all about the affair.
Judge Heacock remarked that there
was much contradictory testimony in
the case and that it came from the de
fendant and his family. "Under the
well known rules of law," he said,
"such testimony is to be received, if at
all. with very great caution."
W. D. Redington, wholesale druggist.
A. H. Vail of Sanborn & Vail and
Richard de Lancie. Deputy United
States Marshal, testified to Leo's good
reputation.
United States Secret Service Agent
George W. Hazen and Assistant United
States Attorney Charles M. Fickert
said they would prove by Police De
tective Gibson that he had arrested
Lee five years ago on a charge of
forgery. The hearing was thereupon
continued until 2 o'clock this after
noon in order that the detective might
be present and testify.
