Considers Horseshoeing Bids.
The Supervisors' joint Committee on
j Health, Electricity and Police yester
day considered the bids filed for the
shoeing of the horses of various city
departments. The different bidders
addressed the committee in support
of their ability to do the work. The
bidders who are members of the Mas
ter Horseshoers' Association all bid
12 60 per horse and $ 1 50 for rubber
pads, claiming that the prices were
fair if the best work is desired. Ed
Toy. who is not a member of the asso
ciation, was the lowest bidder, at $2 25
for shoeing a horse and ft 25 for the
pads. The committee took the matter
i under advisement
GARBAGE MEN
STILL IDLE
Mayor Schmitz Will Make an
| Effort to Settle Strike
at the Reduction Works
RESIDENTS INDIGNANT
Refuse Is Piled Up on Va
cant Lots by Oraer of
the City's Chief Executive
The big garbage crematory at the
foot of Eighth street looks like a de
serted fortress now. The huge piles
of garbage surrounding the place
makefit appear even more suggestive,
and the passerby who is ignorant of
ihe fact that a strike ia in progress
would suppose that these heaps of
rubbish were the breastworks of some
besieged army.
The people of the neighborhood are
up in arms against the city officials for
allowing the garbage to be dumped
near the crematory. But yesterday
they discovered there was no redress,
for the Mayor has granted the scaven
gers permission to dump the refuse
on adjoining vacant lots till the strike
has been settled.
A delegation of the men employed
in the crematory yesterday waited
vainly for Mayor Schmitz to tell him
their troubles. The Mayor will hear
the tales of woe of the men and the
officials of the crematory to-day and
bus already undertaken to straighten
out the trouble.
There was no riot at the crematory
yesterday. A squad of patrolmen par
aded up and down In the vicinity of
the place to keep order. Many of the
strikers were on hand to see what
would happen, but they behaved them
sehrSs and casually looked on, while
wagon after wagon drove up and
dumped its contents on adjoining lots.
The men say they will not go back
till the time clock is done away with.
They have taken a dislike to this piece
of modern machinery and vow they
WW have nothing to do with it. They
also want extra pay for overtime. The
off Vials Bay the demands of the men
will not be granted and that their
Pisces will be filled within the next
U w days.
In the meantime the garbage is fast
piling up in the vacant lots near the
crematory and residents of the district
are wrought up over the nuisance.
The foul smelling garbage is a menace
to the community and unless some
thing is done within the next fqrty
eight hours there is likely to be
trouble.
There are about twenty green hands
at present at work in the crematory.
These men cannot begin to handle half
the stuff that comes in, and, as the
scavengers insist on making quick
time, they simply dump the refuse In
some convenient place and hurry on
their way amid the abuse hurled at
them by the indignant residents of the*
district.
Water Company Files Statement.
The Spring Valley Water Company
yesterday filed with the Supervisors a
statement of its properties and works
now in use for supplying water to the
city. The statement is a voluminous
one, and give 3 a detailed statement
of its properties in San Mateo, San
Francisco and Alameda counties, to
gether with a list of water rights and
rights of way purchased on the Ala
meda Creek system.

The Scientific Alarmist.
The plain citizen 1b booked for all
sorts of trouble if he takes with deep
seriousnessâa habit of the plain citi
zenâall we are hearing these days
about "Impure food." We learned that
our coffee is tainted with some kind
of an acid that just glories in chewing
up the nerves and membranes and fur- <
nishing physicians with new clinical
subjects for the paresis class. The oat
meal contains a liberal percentage of
sawdust secured from trees that grow
in a marsh where the original malaria
germ has its habitat. The milk is a si
lent partner of the typhoid germ and
the rolls are shortened with a lard sub
stitute made from cottonseed oil pro
duced from cotton that is full of boll
weevils. The man who dodges these
and other attendant evils at breakfast
and is allowed to live until dinner finds
prussic acid in his peas, strychnine in
his olives, deadly phosphates in the
soup, sure death in the cheese and
borax in everything. â Washington
Post. ,
Beds Teed by Soldiers.
The beds used by the soldiers of Ger
many and Russia are simple straw
beds, without sheet or mattress, on
which they sleep with a blanket or
over them. In Russia, till recently, the
soldiers slept with their clothes on, on
camp beds, but now the ordinary beds
are being put into use âthe result of
association with more civilized coun
tries. After this it cannot be doubted
that the French soldier's bed is the
best of all. He has a wood or iron bed
stead, a straw bed, a wool mattress,
sheets, a brown woolen coverlet and
an extra quilt for cold weather. Thus
the bed of the French soldier is the
softest of all soldier's beds, as the bed
of the French peasant is acknowledged
to be the best of all in European coun
tries.-!-Indianapolls News.
Shipments of anthracite coal from the ;
mines In the month of May aggregated
5,28Efc079 tons, which is larger than *.n
any month ever reported, except April
of this year and January of last year.
This heavy movement does not include
the usual volume of shipments by lake,
as lake navigation is largely suspended
on account of the strike, and the de
mand is still great.

we find ourselves in smooth waters at |
last."

partment was the father of a state
ment of which the bottom was '
knocked out by those who followed !
him in the second reading debate, yet j
the honorable member for St. Pancras 1
has thought fit to trot It out again." ' j
"Among the many jarring notes I
heard in this house on military affairs j
this subject at least must be regarded
M an oasis."
"The interests of the employers and j
employed are the same nine times out
of tenâl will even say ninety-nine
times out of ten."
"Our*ongues are tied, our hands are
fettered and we are really beating the '
! air to no purpose."
4*l will now repeat what I was about j
jto say when the honorable member in- :
i terrupted me."

bt en a bone of contention among, us." ,

Relief of the Poor.
During 1903 Great Britain spent more
than $61,000,000 fn poor relief. In vari
ous English towns there are now daily
processions, 500 to 1000 strong, of un
employed mens marching through the
streets demanding food or work from
the authorities. In Westham, near |
London, with a population of about i
200,000, 7000 or 8000 men, with families !
dependent on them, are out of work, j
In the United States, while conditions !
are not so bad, the pressure on chari- I
table institutions both public and pri- j
vate is steadily increasing. It is said
that there are more men "out of a j
job" in New York City to-day than i
ever before.
The grave fault of all these remedies
is, according to Mr. Champness, that
they .are aimed at the symptoms rather
than the cause of the disease. It is
like trying to cure a man with a can
cer by giving him opium to relieve the
pain. Society finds a certain number
of its members poor and helpless. It
puts its hand in the-"well-to-do" man's
pocket and takes enough to keep the
paupers from starvation.
Mr. Champness very reasonably con
tends that it could better afford to
spend its money in a scientific hunt for
the cause or causes of pauperism, and
if possible so amend its laws as to de
crease or do away with the worst forms
of poverty.âNew York Globe.
The Size of Texas.
"This will help you to figure out just
how large Texas really is. If you have
a star mathematician in your family,
tell him the number of square miles
there are In the big State, then tell him
the population of the globe; then ask
him if all the people in the world were
placed in Texas and its soil divided out
â Among them per capita, how large
would the man's farm be who had a
wife and two children? When he gets
through figuring, then wnlsper in his
ear: 'More than half an acre.' "âTex
arcana Courier.
Growth of Absentmindness.
There was a time when weddings and
funerals were looked upon as engage
ments which It was impossible to post
pone. Of late, however, the growing
laxity of the age has affected even
these. Menâand women, too âare be
coming so careless that they not only
put off the'r weddings, but now and
then miss the appointment. Some are
so incorrigibly casual that they would
be absent from their own funerals if
they had any volition In the matter. â

New York Stock Market.
NEW YORK. Jan 5.âThers was little life
left in the stock market to-day and the senti
ment as expressed was thoroughly uncertain.
The fluctuations werr constant and the level
of the market shl'ted many times from an
average nigher than last night to a lower
, â¢ cne. Dealings were small snd were attributed
â¢ almost altogether to professional sources. It
was the common remark that there was no
evidence of public participation In the market.
The expected January investment demasid foil
ed to develop and the fear was thus engen
dered that whatever buying was to be ex
pected on this account had been Included In
the remember movement, which was attribut
ed at the time to a demand from dealers for
the purpose of stocking up for the January
investr..ent dtmand. The feeble downward
. moyerr.entf which alternated with the Indif
ferent advances were supposed to be due to
short-celling by traders to a large extent, such
as is the consequence almost invariably of a
dull and halting period in the buying of
0 stocks There was some natural reaction from
the stimulation caused by the fall of Port
Arthur and the Northern Securities decision
' and the tone of the discussion, both here and
. abroad was much less hopeful of a prompt
â â¢ peace 35 the result of the surrender of General
' â¢ Stoessel. Rumors of prospective railroad com
binations and absorptions still attracted atten
tion, but there was less confidence in the
universal tendency of the prospective changes
to harmonize thi railroad situation as a whole
and tbe possibility was canvassed of new
' rivalri-s being awakened instead.
SoriV effect was produced on sentiment by
â¢ rather censorious discussion of recent episodes
in making bank loans for stock market pur
poses, which have come to light in the pro
0 res* of liouidation. The criticism offered by
the New York State Controller In his annual
report of some banking and trust company
â¢ methods are given additional force by such
Further engagements of gold for
export added to the influence of that factor.
MonÂ»y. h"*?ver. continued extremely easy and
served as a safeguard against any pressure
tip'.n the stock market. The general list was
s Â«tained also by a number of points of posi
-trength. Amalgamated Copper bad the
' benefit of a further advance in the price of the
â¢ metal. The coalers proved the main stay of
' the market and it was the strong advance In
. the Eries that made the closing steady.
The bond market was very broad, but the
movement of prices somewhat Irregular. Total

Future Grain and Provisions.
CHICAGO, Jan. s.âThe wheat market opened
firm, with May up a shade to %Â©%c at $1 14%
to $1 14%. The cash situstion in the North
west was the principal cause of the early
: strength. News from Minneapolis was quite
i bullish, a claim being made that 1.600,000
' bushels of wheat had been ordered taken out
'â â  cf ston to go to outside mills. In addition it
! was reported that elevator stocks there yes- â 
terday increased 125,000,000 bushels. Another
' bullish Influence was a cablegram from Ar
gentina stating that the wheat yield in Buenos
jAy res province was considerably below ex
! pectatlons and that general rains were inter
fering with late harvesting. Lower prices at
Liverpool, however, had a depressing effect
on the market here. Reports of the excellent
condition of wheat In the United States also
caused encouragement to bear traders. Many
of the pit traders were apparently bewildered
by the conflicting reports and remained inert.
As a result the volume of trading was rather
small during the flrst part of the day. Later
the market became more active and a firmer
tone developed. May sold up to $1 15%. Dur
ing the last hour of trading*, however, all of
the advance was lost on profit taking. May
acid off to $1 14%, cloalng %6%c lower at
81 14%.
During the entire session the corn market
was subjected to liberal selling by commis
sion houses. May closed at 44% c, a loss of
%Â©%c.
Oats were weak. May closed %c lower at
GOV.
Provisions were weak as a result of renewed
liquidation by local holders. At the close May
pork was down 10612% c, lard was eft 567 %c
and ribs were 2%Â©6 c lower.

Butter. Cheese and Eggs.
General dullness Is the complaint all along
the line, and Butter. Cheese and Egsjs all
show a downward tendency. Butter la about
%c lower and most houses are atill shading
the quotations to sell. Cheese has lately been
dragging and has had to give way %c. Ranch
Eggs have declined more sharply than either
Butter or Cheese and are still slowly accumu
lating, though receipts are not very large.
But as this Is the season of the year for thetn
to decline buyers are holding off In expecta
tion of still lower prices, which increases the
weakness. Store Eggs are meeting with a
better demand than ranch, as during the cool
winter months they are about as good and
are certainly cheaper.
Receipts were 40.700 lbs Butter, 530 cases
Eggs and 27,700 lbs Cheese.

STOCK MARKET.
Continued Activity In Mining Shares,
f)%hir Lead Inc.
Trading on the Stock and Bond Exchange
continued light, most of the business being in
bonds. There were no variations worthy of
note. The Comatock mining shares were again
very active on reported finds In Ophlr. but
though quotations moved up and down ' con
siderably the net changes for the day were
narrow. The Tonopahs were quiet In the
morning, but more active In tbe afternoon,
with no pronounced fluctuations. The oil
stocks continued inactive.
The total dividends paid an oil stocks listed
on the California Stock and Oil Exchange for
December. 1904, were $106,000, making a
grand total to date of $5,714,206.
The Kern River Oil Company has declared
a dividend of 10c per share, or $2000. payable |
January 16.
Dividends paid yesterday: Paciflc Lighting
Company, regular monthly 35c per share,
amounting to $14,000: Hawaiian Commercial .
and Sugar Company. 50c per share, amounting I
to $50,000: Onomea Sugar Company, regular I
monthly. 20c per share, amounting to $10.000. !
The California-atreet Cable Railroad Com
pany will pay s regular monthly dividend of ,
90c per share January 11. This Is an increase
of 15c per share over the rate heretofore paid.
The Paciflc States Telephone and Telegraph
Company will pay a regular quarterly dividend
of $1 75 per share January 16.
The First National Bank of San Frane'aco
has declared a regular semi-annual dividend
at the rate of 10 per cent per annum. Thia
with the extra Christmas dividend, makes 14
per cent for the past year.
The Osceola Copper Mining Company has de- '
dared a semi-annual dividend of $2 per share, !
payable January 27. This is $1 per share
more than pais! aix months ago.

FURNISHES MATERIAL
FOR CAREFUL STUDY
Electric Corporation Issues Interest
ins Map of the Central Part
of California.
The California Gas and Electrlo
Corporation has issued a large map of
Central California lithograohed in re
lief, showing: in a most comprehensive
manner the county lines, rivers, roads,
cities, towns and railway lines. The
map is also platted with the water sys
tems of the California Gas and Elec-
I trie Corporation and shows the loca
tion of the company's various power
houses and distributing stations and
its pole lines, which stretch over the
greater portion of the central part of
the State.
The map is one of the most interest
ing studies of what is being done in
Central California in distributing elec
tricity for power and lighting yurw
poses.

Australians Entertain.
The Australian Coo-ec Club grave a
splendid entertainment last night at
Odd Fellows' Hall. Dr. Isidore Myers
was in the Chair. The whole of the
programme was given by Australians,
ihe entertainers being J. H. Pollard.
F. J. Frair. M. Michelson. Dr. Nagel.
O. Rices, W. W. Willis, S. Goldstein.
Madame Cas-Hamber, Miss Violet
Kohn and Miss M. London. The Coo
ee Club decided to visit Fischer's
Theater next Thursday night in honor
of Oliver Ashton, an Australian who
is now appearing there, and on the
second Thursday in February the
members will again meet at Odd Fel
lows' Hall.

LURING THEM
Drs. Poheim and Drucker and
John O'Brien Are Strug
gling for Appointment
A PRETTY FIGHT IS OX
Mayor Favorable to Rela
tive and Friend, but Medi
cos Are Strongly Backed
A pretty three-cornered fight was j
j being waged yesterday for the Po- j
! lice Commissionershlp which becomes I
! vacant next Monday owing to the ex
; piration of the term of Police Com- !
' missloner Howell. The three men who |
appear to be the most formidable can- j
1 didates for the post are John O'Brien, j
I a salesman for Roth & Co., the j
j liquor merchants; Dr. Joseph Poheim
of the Board of Health and Dr. George
Drucker, former member of the Board j
!of Education and ex-member of the
j Grand Jury.
John O'Brien is a new Richmond in
! the field and is related to the Mayor
jby marriage. The Mayor is desirous
iof having a personal friend on each
of the municipal boards and com-
I missions, and the Police Commission
lis the only one upon which such is
I not the case. His Honor is very kind
ly disposed toward O'Brien and the
i family influence is being strongly ex- \
! erted in O'Brien's behalf.
On the other hand the friends of i
Dr. Poheim have not lost all hope ,
that he will be the next Police Com
missloner notwithstanding the Mayor's j
recent declaration that he would re- j
appoint Poheim on the Board of j
Health. Poheim has the backing of
Abe Ruef for the office and political |
pressure of the strongest kind is be- j
ing exerted in his behalf.
Then again the friends of Dr. \
George Drucker are making valiant
efforts to secure the plum for their |
favorite. In fact one of the political
powers acknowledged that Drucker j
has brought Influences to bear thati
cannot be ignored. Drucker is strong
ly backed by the "Horses and Carts," '
which wing of the Democratic party
has rendered good service to Mayor
Schmitz in the hour of need. This is <
the situation oh the Police Commis- j
sionership fight as it stood yesterday, j
although Arthur Dudley Cross and G. j
M. Roy are said to be still in the
j running.
Thomas F. Egan still seems to have
j the inside track for Commissioner of ;
j Public Works and things look rosy j
I for William H. Metson for the Park
l Commissionershlp. Commissioners
Altmann. Wreden, Voorsanger and
Rogers will be reappointed and Alex
ander O'Grady Is slated for secretary i
of the Police Commission.
Royal Arch Holds Installation.
San Francisco Lodge No. 2 of the 1
Knights of the Royal Arch held Its an
nual Installation Wednesday night in
Eagles' Hall. Grand Valiant Commander
I Theodore Lunstedt was the presiding
officer at the ceremonies. The annual
report showed that there were more
than 1000 members belonging to the I
order and that there was $10,000 in
the treasury'- The next State conven
tion of the order will be held In Sac
ramento in April of this year.

THE SAN FKANIISLQ CALL, FRIDAY. JANUARY 6, 1905.
BLANCHE BATES
AS PLAYWRIGHT
Accomplished Actress Ap
pears in Own Clever Come
dietta "3ly Aunt's Advice"
PLAYLET FULL OF FUN
Versatility Shown in Her
Production at Same Mati
nee of "Madame Butterfly"
Blanche Bates made her debut as
I playwright yesterday afternoon at the
Grand Opera-house, when her come
dietta, "My Aunt's Advice," written in
collaboration with George Arliss, was
1 presented. The piece is in one act and
j highly amusing, though it fails to con- I
' vince. The story is a good one, but !
i the vague drawing of one of the three
I characters concerned makes it largely j
implausible,
j Mr. and Mrs. Middleton, married j
three months, and Dick Richards, a i
i guest of the house for the same length |
of time, are discovered. Mrs. Middle- j
ton is tired of the situation and is de- j
sirous of getting rid of the intruder
in the conjugal tete-a-tete. The stu- j
pidity and rudeness of the friend âMr.
Ormonde makes him both stupid and
rude in the beffhiningârenders Mrs. j
Middleton's desire explicable. Her hus
band's desire for his continual com- i
pany is less easily explainable. Mr. and
' Mrs. Middleton have "words'* about the j
i man. But Mrs. Middleton has an aunt j
! who knows things. To her the young ;
j wife has confided the situation. Louisa j
J âthe auntâadviseB a flirtation with the
I gentleman after the manner of a cer- i
j tain chapter by Le Sage In the "His
| tory of Gonzales." This by letter.
Mrs. Middleton, seeing that her hus-
I band is Impossible of conviction, pro- i
I ceeds to take the advice of aunt. She J
! confesses, a la Gonzales, an overpower- j
; ing passion for her husband's friend, i
i bidding him "Fly!" Very funny is the
| friend's reception of the announce- |
| ment as done by Mr. Ormonde, still j
1 as the stupid though now polite friend.
He promises to fly. with a delightfully J
â  complacent glance in the mirror at his j
fatal beauty. But after Mrs. Middle
too has left the room he picks up and .
; reads Aunt Louisa's explanatory let- ]
ter! Here he becomes the cad. Then j
j he plans revenge in a humorous and j
! Imaginative fashion quite at variance j
! with what has heretofore been seen of j
j him. That kind of man would not .
I have been third in a honeymoon.
Still, when Mrs. Middleton returns,
and Richards, whom she had thought
gone, returns to make a burning dec
laration of his hidden passion, in
phrase a la Dumas the younger, the
situation is distinctly hilarious. Mean
time her husband has demanded an ex- j
planation of his friend's departure. !
Mrs. Middleton has insinuated Rich- !
ards* guilty passion. Middleton then
follows the supposed would-be de
stroyer of his peace. He returns to find
Richards In the middle of his mock;
declaration. But why Mr, Richards j
should imagine that Middleton would j
see through the pretense gets me. The
curtain, however, goes down on Mrs.
Middleton with her head on Mr. M.'s |
I shoulder and Richards waving them
a valedictory adieu. Yet one laughed all
j through.
Blanche Bates, as Mrs. Middleton.
was welcome in her good American
gown, again looked charming and did j
I everything for the part. Mr. Or- >
monde also did what he could and Mr. j
McGrane as Middleton filled in satis
factorily.
It was a far cry from this to the
"Madame Butterfly" that followed, the
"Madame Butterfly" with which
Blanche Bates has been so famously j
connected. Anew, the play touched, de- j
lighted, thrilled. It is a little classic,
and Miss Bates' Cho Oho San a thing j
of sheer beauty. The actress was un- J
fortunate yesterday in Baby Gertrude !
Slavin's defection. A small yelp from j
the wings was all of Miss Slavin that
made its appearance. The piece was j
played without "Baby Trouble," and
those that have seen it will understand j
the unfortunate difference.
But Miss Bates was a delight
throughout. Her performance was !
cameo-like in Its finish and fine- j
ness. Yet there was color, exquisite
color. She realized with happiest In
stinct the tielicate pathos, gayety, ra- !
cial charm of the character. Its touch
ing patience, sweetness, faith, dignity,
delightful coquetry were all pictured
with a. sure and admirably reserved
art. But one forgot the art In the ex- .
quislte humanness of the portrait. It'
was not Blanche Bates before us, but a j
little butterfly of a "Japanese wife." !
who happened to have a soul. We gave
tears to her grief, and as many, per- j
haps, to her pathetic faith in the man
that had deserted her. You will hardly ! 1
find a thing more artistic, more subtly
realistic, or more charming than Miss ;
Bates' Madame Butterfly. ! <
Ada Lewis was a distinct addition to
the illusion as Suzuki, the maid; Mr. !
Ormonde's Sharpless was wholly in the
picture, and Albert Brunlng, as the ',
would-be Japanese husband, was a sat- \
Isfying brown man. The American
wife, with Miss Leslie Preston, came
Just as crudely as It always does. But, i
then, that was not Miss Preston's fault I

prise at their stupidity in so constru- â¢
ing the ordinance.

WINE MAKERS
OBJECT TO TAX
Â»
California Viticultural Club
Petitions Authorities to
Help the Young Industry
The California Viticultural Club held
an animated meeting yesterday with
the idea of taking steps to protest
against the proposed internal revenue
tax on sweet wines, which Commis
sioner Yerkes has recommended in his
annual report.
A set of resolutions embodying the
protest was drawn up and copies were
forwarded to the President of the
Senate and to the Speaker of the As
sembly at Sacramento with the re
quest that the same be adopted by
both branches of the Legislature. The
resolutions follow:
Whereas, Commissioner Yerkes of the In
ternal Revenue Bureau, In his annual report
to the Congress of the United States recom
mends the it-vying of a minimum tax upon the
grape brandy now used in the fortification of
sweet wine manufactured within the United
States amounting to 25 cents per proof gallon,
and which tax Is equivalent to not less than
5 bents per wine gallon on the sweet wine
manufactured; and
Whereas, California being essentially a grape
producing State and the largest producer of
sweet wines manufactured within the United
States*, and
Whereas, The imposition of such a tax would
work incalculable Injury to an Industry In
which there la Involved over $80,000,000, and
which amount was invested with the Inspira
tion that the sweet wins laws of the nation
would be continued In their Integrity; and
Whereas, The Industrial business Interests of
California, In conventions assembled, have
strongly protested against such a tax, declaring
that Its Imposition will virtually destroy the
sweet wine Industry of California, and have
appealed to the Legislature to strengthen the
protests an a Inst such a tax; therefore be it
Resolved, That the Senate and Assembly
of the State of California concur In these ex
pressions of opinion and recommend Cali
fornia's representation in Congress to ener
getically oppose the enactment of such a meas
urc, and further to petition the President of
the United States, Theodore Roosevelt, to
veto any law levying such a tax, as an enact
ment of that character would injure the busi
ness Interests of California many millions of
lollars and wipe out the viticultural interests
vt this State and retard the State's progress,
while no material Increase can accrue to the
revenue of the General Government by ths
Imposition of a tax upon sweet wines manu
factured within the United States, as the busi
ness from which such tax Is expected will
sntlrely disappear; and be it further
Resolved, That the Governor of the State
jf California. George C. Pardee, be and he
s hereby requested to have transmitted to
he President of the United States, to each
Senator and Congressman representing the
State of California in the national Congress
i copy of this petition st the earliest pos
ilble moment.
ANIMATED MEETING.
The meeting was an animated one.
rohn Swett presided and E. H. Twight.
vas secretary. Nearly everybody pres
ent took a hand in the proceedings, and
here was but one opinion expressed re
rarding the proposed tax.
The members were nearly all practi
:al grape growers and manufacturers
>f wine. They declared that the Droar
â¢ess of viticulture in this State would
>c destroyed by the adoption of Com
nissioner Yerkes' recommendation
hat a minimum tax of 25 cents a proof
rallon be levied upon the grape brandy
ised in the fortification of sweet wines,
rhis would be equivalent to not less
han 5 cents a wine gallon on the sweet
REV. W. M. REILLY IS
CONFINED TO HIS HOME
Rector of St. Paul's Church Suffering
From Indisposition, but His Con
dition Is Not Serious.
The Rev. W. M. Reilly, rector of
St. Paul's Church, California street
near Fillmore, has been indisposed for
some days and since his sermon on
j Christmas day has been confined to
his room.
On Christmas eve the Rev. Mr.
j Reilly was the recipient of a
j beautiful gold watch, which was pre-
I sented to him by Miss Beatrice Fife
jon behalf of the parishioners. Ac
-1 companying the watch was a parch
ment, on the upper corner of which
is a water color miniature of St. Paul's
Church, designed by Harry Hedger,
bearing the inscription: "Presented to
Dr. William Maxwell Reilly this
Christmas eve, December the twenty
fourth, in the year of our Lord nine
teen hundred and four, by his parish
ioners in token of their esteem and af
fection."
Mrs. Reilly last night said that the
Rev. Mr. Reilly was much improved,
although still confined to the house.

Father Charles Lathrop Lectures.
Father Charles N. Lathrop gave the
first of a series of four lectures under
the auspices of the Catholic Club last
I night at the Occidental Hotel. He
used for his subject "The History of
the Mass and the Reservation of the
Blessed Sacrament." Edward Adams,
the president of the club, acted as
chairman of the meeting.
+ *
wines manufactured. California is the
leading sweet-wine-making State in
the Union. Sherry and port are the
leading wines In this production.
Sherry is largely used by druggists in
compounding their medicines and is
also largely used for medicinal pur
poses. Brandy is one of* the prominent
factors in the production of these
wines.
BRANDY IN SWEET WINE.
About three-fourths of. the brandy
produced in this State is used in the
production of sweet wine. Brandy is
taxed at the rate of $110 per gallon of
the alcohol contained in it. For the
purpose, however, of aiding the domes
tic sweet-wine industry, the statute re
mits the tax on brandy used in forti- i
fying sweet wine. In his recent reDort.
however, the Commissioner of Internal
Revenue recommends the reimoosition
of the tax on brandy used in the man
ufacture of sweet wines, or at least that
there be collected a minimum tax of 25 j
cents per proof gallon of the alcohol in |
the brandy.
Winemakers say that this would re
sult in a tax of $1,000,000 per annum. ;
and this young industry at its present '
volume cannot stand it.

GADSKI CHARMS
WITH VARIED
PROGRAMME
The second of the Gadski .recital
drew an even larger audience than th'
first to the Alhambra last evening
Both were more than good for the tim<
o' year. Last night's programme lacke<
much of being as the first evening's
Naturally, also, the singer was not ii
quite the inspired mood of Tuesda:
night. But when the gods give us Gad
j ski in any mood we may be thankful.
By the way. My bad script is doubt
less responsible for my being made t<
say in Wednesday morning's criticisn
that Gadski's voice lacks power. Wha
I did say was that Gadski's highei
notes lack fullness and quality. Per
haps this was even more discernible
last evening, one feeling the voca
strain when there was any demand foi
climax in the far upper range. It seemi
that since her appearance here in open
these notes have somewhat suffered
but there is a corresponding gain ir
the remarkable beauty of the lowei
compass. But it is a beautiful voice
and how she is blessed In its use!
Gadski did not begin really to "sing'
last night until her encore to the sec
ond group of songs, the "Still as th<
Night." The usual aria from "Samsor
et Delila" preceded this, handsomelj
enough sung. She gave then a grouj
of Brahms lieder, that included thÂ«
"Feldeinsamkeit," the lovely "Vor,
Ewiger Liebe," and the "Slumbei
Song"âcharmingly given. Part second
of the programme was devoted tc
American composers. Perhaps it was
this that rather indisposed the singer!
They were not our best songs, though,
Madame,and they came humbly enough
between Brahms and Wolf. Parker's
"Hora Novlssima" is, however, an aria
to arouse patriotism. It is a splendid
song and was admirably treated by the
singer. Hammond's "Cupid Wings"
was rather amazing fare on a Gadski
programme, and amazingly, also, was
encored. The Rider-Possart "Christ
mas Night" followed. Then came
MacDowell's "The Clover" and a simple
and charming little ballad, "Airly Bea
con," also encored. Nevin's "Night
ingale Song" followed and was encored,
After that Madame began to sing!
Her "Still as the Night" was adorably
sung, palpitating with the essential
musical thrill. So was the Wolf "Ver
borgenheit," the delightful "Kinder
li*der" of Taubert with their artless
humor; the two Strauss songs, "Traum
Durch die Dammerung" and "Caecilie."
She was finally encored and gave a
stunning "Erlkonig," though not with
